Disliked Acts in Wudu: Makruh Tahrimi, Makruh Tanzihi, and Extravagance
Defining makruh and beginning the list of six disliked acts in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Makruh Tahrimi and Makruh Tanzihi in Hanafi fiqh, and apply the ruling on extravagance (israf) in wudu to different water sources.

Core Idea
Extravagance in wudu water is Makruh Tahrimi—closer to haram; with endowed water, it becomes outright haram.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: not all 'disliked' acts are equal. It defines the two types of makruh—tahrimi and tanzihi—and then applies this framework to the first of six disliked acts in wudu: extravagance in water. By understanding the basis for the classification (the strength of the evidence), you will learn how a seemingly minor act can have different rulings depending on the source of water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of makruh
The passage defines Makruh Tahrimi as that which leaving it is wajib, established by zanni evidence, and its doer deserves a deterrent like deprivation of intercession. Makruh Tanzihi is better to leave but not blameworthy. Note the difference in evidence strength.
2
Understand the basis for distinction
The classification depends on whether the evidence is a zanni prohibition (for tahrimi) or merely indicates non-committal avoidance (for tanzihi). This is key for evaluating other acts.
3
Apply to the case of israf
Extravagance in pouring water is ruled as Makruh Tahrimi even with river or owned water, based on the hadith 'What is this extravagance, O Sa'd?' and reports about 'stealing in water'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad considers makruh tahrimi as haram but does not call it so due to lack of explicit text. The well-known view is that it is closer to haram. This is a legitimate difference within the school.
5
Identify the exception for waqf water
When water is endowed specifically for purification (e.g., school wells), extravagance becomes outright haram because it violates the endowment's conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المكروهات (و) مما يكره للمتوضىء ستة أشياء لأنه للتقريب منها (الإسراف صب (الماء) لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لسعد لما مر به وهو يتوضأ : ما هذا السرف يا سعده فقال) كما في المكروهات يقال : كره الشيء يكرهه من باب سمع كرهاً ويضم وكراهية بالتخفيف، والتشديد إذا لم يحبه قاموس، والمكروه عند الفقهاء نوعان: مكروه تحريماً وهو المحمل عند إطلاقهم الكراهة، وهو ما تركه واجب ويثبت بما يثبت به الواجب كما في الفتح ومكروه تنزيهاً وهو تركه أولى من فعله وكثيراً ما يطلقونه فلا بد من النظر في الدليل فإن كان نهياً ظنياً يحكم بكراهة التحريم ما لم يوجد صارف عنه إلى التنزيه، وإن لم يكن الدليل نهياً، بل كان مفيداً للترك الغير الجازم، فهي تنزيهية قاله صاحب البحر ثم المكروه تنزيهاً إلى الحل أقرب اتفاقاً في استحسان البرهان وأما المكروه تحريماً فعند محمد هو حرام، ولم يطلقه عليه لعدم النص الصريح فيه والمشهور عنهما أنه إلى الحرام أقرب بمعنى أنه ليس فيه عقوبة بالنار، بل بغيرها كحرمان الشفاعة وفي التلويح من بحث الفقه المكروه تحريماً يستحق فاعله محذوراً دون العقوبة بالنار، كحرمان الشفاعة، والواجب في رتبة المكروه تحريماً اهـ وقال الزيلعي : من بحث حرمة الخيل القريب من الحرام ما تعلق به محذور دون إستحقاق العقوبة بالنار، بل العتاب كترك السنة المؤكدة فإنه لا يتعلق به عقوبة النار ولكن يتعلق به الحرمان من شفاعة النبي المختار قوله: (ضد المحبوب مراده ما يعم المحبوب الواجب لتدخل كراهة التحريم . قوله : ( والأدب) فيه منافاة لما قدمه أوّل الأداب من أن الأدب لا يلام على تركه، ومن جملته عدم التكلم والإستعانة، وجعل الكراهة هنا تقابله وفيها اللوم وجعل الإستعانة، والتكلم بكلام الناس مكروهين فليتأمل قوله فلا حصر لها تفريع على قوله : فيكره للمتوضيء وقوله ستة أشياء بالنصف بالنظر للشرح لأنه معمول لقوله بعدها قوله : (لأنه للتقريب) أي عدها ستة للتقريب للمبتدىء قوله : (الإسراف في صب الماء الإسراف العمل فوق الحاجة الشرعية في فتاوي الحجة يكره صب الماء في الوضوء زيادة على العدد المسنون، والقدر المعهود لما ورد في الخبر شرار أمتي الذين يسرقون في صب الماء اهـ وفي الدر، ويكره الإسراف فيه تحريماً لو بماء النهر ، أو المملوك له. أما الموقوف على من يتطهر به، ومنه ماء المدارس فحرام اهـ.
Regarding disliked acts: And among what is disliked for the one performing wudu are six things, because they are for approximation from them: (Extravagance in pouring water) due to his saying to Sa'd when he passed by him while performing wudu: 'What is this extravagance, O Sa'd?' He said: as in al-Makruhat. It is said: kariha al-shay'a yakrahuhu from the category of sami', karhan (with damma) and karahiyya with tashdid and without, when he does not like it. Al-Makruh according to the jurists is of two types: Makruh Tahrimi, which is intended when they use the word makruh unconditionally, and it is that which leaving it is obligatory, and it is established by what establishes the obligatory, as in al-Fath. And Makruh Tanzihi, which is that leaving it is better than doing it, and often they use it, so it is necessary to consider the evidence; if it is a zanni prohibition, it is judged as makruh tahrimi as long as there is no factor turning it to tanzih. If the evidence is not a prohibition but merely indicates non-committal avoidance, then it is tanzihi. This was said by the author of al-Bahr. Then Makruh Tanzihi is closer to permissibility, by agreement in the approval of al-Burhan. As for Makruh Tahrimi, according to Muhammad it is haram, but he did not call it that due to the lack of explicit text; and the well-known from the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that it is closer to haram, meaning that there is no punishment of fire, but rather other forms like deprivation of intercession. In al-Talwih from fiqh research: one who does Makruh Tahrimi deserves a deterrent without punishment by fire, like deprivation of intercession, and the obligatory is in the rank of Makruh Tahrimi. End. Al-Zayla'i said: From research on horses, 'close to haram' is that which involves a deterrent without deserving punishment by fire, but rather blame, like leaving the Sunnah Mu'akkada, for which there is no punishment by fire but deprivation of the Prophet's intercession. His saying: (opposite of loved) his intention includes the loved obligatory to include karahah of tahrim. His saying: (and refinement) there is contradiction with what he presented at the beginning of the adab that refinement is not blamed for leaving it, and from it are not speaking and not seeking help, and he made karahah here opposite to that, involving blame, and considered seeking help and speaking worldly talk as makruh. So let it be considered. His saying: 'there is no limit to them' is a branch of his saying: 'it is disliked for the one performing wudu'. And his saying 'six things' is with nasb (accusative) due to the explanation because it is the object of his saying after it. His saying: (because it is for approximation) meaning he counted six for approximation for the beginner. His saying: (extravagance in pouring water) extravagance is action beyond the legal need. In Fatawa al-Hujja, it is disliked to pour water in wudu in excess of the sunnah number and the accustomed amount, due to the report: 'The worst of my community are those who steal in pouring water.' End. In al-Durr, extravagance is disliked as tahrimi, even if with river water or owned water. As for water endowed for purification, like water of schools, it is haram.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the section of disliked acts (makruhat) in wudu from Maraqi al-Falah. The commentator first defines the term 'makruh' linguistically and legally. Legally, it splits into two categories: Makruh Tahrimi, which is closer to haram and its doer deserves a deterrent like deprivation of intercession, and Makruh Tanzihi, which is merely not better, leaving it is preferable. The distinction is based on the strength of the evidence: if the evidence is a zanni (probable) prohibition, it is tahrimi unless there is a factor turning it to tanzih; if the evidence merely indicates avoidance, it is tanzihi. This is crucial for students to understand the gradations of rulings beyond the five categories. The text then lists the first of six disliked acts: extravagance (israf) in pouring water. Extravagance is using water beyond the sunnah count (e.g., more than three washes per limb) or beyond the necessary amount. The hadith of the Prophet criticizing Sa'd for extravagance is cited. The ruling: it is Makruh Tahrimi even with river or owned water; if the water is endowed specifically for purification (e.g., school wells), then using it extravagantly is actually haram because it violates the endowment's conditions. This shows the interaction between fiqh rules and waqf conditions. The commentator also notes a subtlety about the term 'adab' (refinement) used earlier: calling some conditions 'adab' implies no blame for leaving them, but here in disliked acts there is blame. This apparent contradiction is left for reflection.

Key Terms
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahrimi): A legally disliked act that is close to haram; its doer deserves a deterrent short of hellfire, e.g., deprivation of intercession.
مكروه تنزيهاً (makruh tanzihi): A legally disliked act that is better left; no blame, but not recommended.
إسراف (israf): Extravagance; using water in wudu beyond the sunnah quantity or the necessary amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on using more water than needed in wudu from a river?
A) Halal, because river water is abundant.
B) Makruh Tanzihi, because leaving it is preferable.
C) Makruh Tahrimi, closer to haram.
D) Haram, regardless of the water source.

Key Takeaway
Extravagance in wudu water is Makruh Tahrimi (closer to haram), and with endowed water it becomes outright haram; the distinction between tahrimi and tanzihi hinges on the strength of the evidence.

Six Disliked Acts in Wudu (Makruhat al-Wudu)
Defines makruh and lists six disliked acts with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six disliked acts in wudu according to the Hanafi school, identify the type of dislike (tahrimi or tanzihi) for each, and explain the primary evidence for israf and isti'ana, including the intra-madhhab dispute on the latter.

Core Idea
Extravagance in wudu is disliked even from a flowing river; stinginess is equally blameworthy.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi defines the category of makruh and enumerates six acts disliked in wudu. Understanding these acts helps the believer refine his worship, avoiding extremes of waste and stinginess, and recognizing that even small actions like asking for water can affect the completeness of one's devotion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six disliked acts
From the text, list the six acts: israf (extravagance), taqtir (stinginess), striking the face with water, speaking during wudu, seeking help without excuse, and wiping the head thrice with one water.
The sixth act is inferred from the commentary about 'tathlith al-mash bi ma' wahid'.
2
Determine the type of karahah for each act
Most acts are considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) according to the relied-upon view, except striking the face which is tanzihi (mildly disliked). Note the intra-madhhab dispute: Imam Muhammad holds makruh tahrimi as haram, while the Sahibayn consider it close to haram.
The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the primary proof for israf
The hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas where the Prophet (peace be upon him) criticized his use of water even from a flowing river. This establishes that israf is blameworthy and disliked, regardless of water availability.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is definitive (zanni al-thubut, qati al-dalala).
4
Examine the dispute on seeking help (isti'ana)
The text reports that seeking help is disliked based on a hadith of Umar, but then cites Imam al-Wabari who permits it, noting that the hadith is weak. The commentary clarifies that if there is a genuine need, it is allowed.
This shows the later Hanafi practice of preferring ease when evidence is weak.
5
Synthesize the lesson's thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference lies in the ruling on seeking help: the strict view discourages it, while the permissive view allows it when needed. Also, the distinction between makruh tahrimi and haram affects the sinfulness of the act.
The mufta bihi for isti'ana is that it is permissible if there is a need, as per the more accommodating view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
في المكروهات (و) مما ( يكره) المكروه ضد المحبوب، والأدب فيكره (للمتوضى) ضد ما استحب من الآداب فلا حصر لها بعدها (ستة أشياء) لأنه للتقريب منها (الإسراف صب (الماء) لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لسعد لما مر به وهو يتوضأ : ما هذا السرف يا سعده فقال : أفي الوضوء سرف قال : نعم وإن كنت على نهر جار ومنه تثليث المسح بماء حديد (والتقتير) بجعل الغسل مثل المسح (فيه) لأن فيه تفويت السنة وقال عليه السلام: «خير الأمور أوساطها» (و) يكره ضرب الوجه به لمنافاته شرف الوجه فيلقيه برفق عليه (و) يكره التكلم بكلام الناس لأنه يشغله عن الأدعية (و) يكره الإستعانة بغيره) لقول عمر رضي الله عنه رأيت رسول الله لا يستقي ماء لوضوئه فبادرت أن أستقي له فقال : مه يا عمر فإني لا أريد أن يعينني على صلاتي أحد من غير عذر لأن الضرورات تبيح المحظورات فكيف بما لا حظر فيه، وعن الإمام الوبري أنه لا بأس به فإن الخادم كان يصب على

قوله: (فقال أفي الوضوء سرف) الذي في رواية أحمد وأبي يعلى والبيهقي في شعبه، وابن ماجه في سننه فقال : أو في الوضوء بزيادة الواو العاطفة على مقدر تقديره أتقول هذا وفي الوضوء سرف. قوله : (والتقتير) هو عدم بلوغ الحد المسنون، فلو اقتصر على ما دون الثلاث قيل: يأثم، وقيل لا وقيل يأثم بالاعتياد واعلم إنه نقل غير واحد الإجماع على عدم التقدير في ماء الوضوء والغسل بل هو بقدر الكفاية لإختلاف طباع الناس، وعن عائشة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الغسل من الجنابة صاع ثمانية أرطال وفي الوضوء رطلان اهـ. وهما مد فالمد ربع الصاع . جرت السنة

قوله: (يجعل الغسل مثل (المسح بأن يقرب الغسل إلى حد الدهن لكن لا بد من أن يقطر، ولو قطرتين حتى يكون غسلاً وإلا فلا يصح الوضوء أصلاً. قوله: (ويكره ضرب الوجه) أي تنزيهاً، ومثله غيره من بقية الأعضاء كما في الدر. قوله: (لمنافاته شرف الوجه) ولأن فيه إنتضاح غسالة الماء المستعمل، فالتحرز عنها ،أولى ولا يغمض عينيه ولا يقبض فمه شديداً بحيث تنكتم حمرة الشفتين ومحاجر العينين أي أطراف الأجفان، ومنابت الهدب لوجوب إيصال الماء إلى ذلك المحل حتى لو بقيت منه لمعة لم يصبها الماء لا يصح الوضوء كما في الحلبي.

قوله: (فيلقيه برفق (عليه أي يرسل الماء على الوجه من أعلى الجبهة برفق، ثم يدلكه به قوله : (ويكره التكلم بكلام الناس ما لم يكن لحاجة تفوته بتركه قاله ابن أمير حاج. قوله : لأنه يشغله عن الأدعية ولأجل تخليص الوضوء من شوائب الدنيا لأنه مقدمة العبادة وذكر بعض العارفين أن الاستحضار في الصلاة يتبع الإستحضار في الوضوء، وعدمه في عدمه. قوله : (ويكره الإستعانة الخ) تقدم ما فيه وإنه لا بأس بها وأما حديث عمر فضعيف ولا يقاوي غيره مما يدل على ثبوتها عنه ، أفاده بعض المحققين.
Book of Purification. Regarding the disliked acts (makruhat). (And) from what is disliked: makruh is the opposite of beloved, and etiquette is disliked for the one performing wudu (opposite of what is recommended of etiquettes, so there is no limit after them) (six things) because it is for approximation (extravagance in pouring water) due to his statement, peace be upon him, to Sa'd when he passed by him while he was performing wudu: 'What is this extravagance, O Sa'd?' He said: 'Is there extravagance in wudu?' He said: 'Yes, even if you are on a flowing river.' And from it: wiping three times with a single water (and stinginess) by making the washing like the wiping (in it) because it wastes the sunnah, and he said, peace be upon him: 'The best of affairs is their middle.' (And) disliked is striking the face with it because it contradicts the honor of the face, so he should pour it gently upon it. (And) disliked is speaking the speech of people because it distracts from supplications. (And) disliked is seeking help from others) due to the statement of Umar, may Allah be pleased with him: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah not fetching water for his wudu, so I hastened to fetch water for him, and he said: Wait, O Umar, for I do not want anyone to assist me in my prayer without an excuse,' because necessities permit prohibitions, so how about what is not prohibited? And from Imam al-Wabari that it is fine because the servant used to pour for him...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi defines the legal category of makruh and lists six disliked acts in wudu. The author begins by stating that makruh is the opposite of mahbub (beloved), and that certain etiquettes are disliked for the one performing wudu. The six things are enumerated as a simplification for beginners. The first is extravagance (israf) in using water, supported by the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas where the Prophet criticized him even though he was on a flowing river. The commentary notes that israf in wudu is makruh tahrimi even with one's own water, and haram if the water is endowed for that purpose. The second is taqtir (stinginess), meaning washing insufficiently like wiping, which violates the sunnah. The third is striking the face with water, which disrespects the face; water should be poured gently. The fourth is talking during wudu as it distracts from supplications. The fifth is seeking help without need, based on a hadith where the Prophet refused assistance. However, the commentary cites Imam al-Wabari that it is permissible if needed. The sixth is mentioned in the earlier text as 'three wipings with one water' which the commentary explains as wiping the head three times with a single water. Each disliked act is explained with proofs and some intra-madhhab differences.

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makruh): That which is disliked; leaving it is superior to doing it. It is divided into makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked).
الإسراف (al-israf): Using water beyond the legal need; more than the sunnah amount or more than necessary for cleanliness.
التقتير (al-taqtir): Washing so sparingly that it does not fulfill the sunnah; making washing like wiping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six disliked acts in wudu according to this passage?
A) Extravagance in using water
B) Wiping the head thrice with one water
C) Striking the face with water
D) Speaking during wudu

Key Takeaway
The six disliked acts in wudu help the believer balance between waste and insufficiency, maintaining the sunnah and avoiding extremes.

Disliked Acts in Wudu: Balancing Between Israf and Taqtir
Explaining rulings on disliked actions during wudu with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the legal rulings (makruh tanzihi) for israf, taqtir, striking the face, speaking, and seeking help during wudu, citing the Hanafi proofs and relevant distinctions.

Core Idea
Wudu requires balance: using too much or too little water, and unnecessary actions, are disliked but not invalidating.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi presents a series of rulings on disliked (makruh) acts in wudu. Understanding these helps you refine your practice, avoiding extremes while preserving the sunnah. The author distinguishes between what is merely disliked and what invalidates wudu, providing both proof and practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked act
List the four main actions described: israf (extravagance), taqtir (using too little water), striking the face, speaking worldly talk, and seeking help without excuse. Note that each has a different basis.
The text also mentions related gestures like closing eyes too tightly.
2
Examine the proof for each ruling
For israf and taqtir, the hadith 'The best of matters are their middles' is cited. For striking the face, the reason is its nobility and the splash of used water. For speaking, it distracts from supplications. For seeking help, the hadith of Umar is presented but noted as weak.
The author balances textual evidence (hadith) with rational considerations (ma'qul).
3
Understand the gradation of dislikedness
All these are makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful unless habitual). However, some acts like taqtir can invalidate wudu if the water does not drip at all.
The commentator clarifies that 'striking the face' is only disliked, while 'speaking' is disliked only when not necessary.
4
Analyze the dispute on seeking help
Imam al-Wabari permits it absolutely, and some later scholars prefer permissibility. The author notes the hadith of Umar is weak, so the stronger view is that it is not disliked if needed.
This illustrates how the Hanafi school handles conflicting evidence.
5
Apply the rulings to modern scenarios
Consider everyday situations: using a dripping tap, splashing water on the face, talking during wudu, or having someone pour water for you. Each has a clear ruling based on this text.
The key is intention and avoiding extremes.

الجنابة صاع ثمانية أرطال وفي الوضوء رطلان اهـ. وهما مد فالمد ربع الصاع . جرت السنة قوله: (يجعل الغسل مثل (المسح بأن يقرب الغسل إلى حد الدهن لكن لا بد من أن يقطر، ولو قطرتين حتى يكون غسلاً وإلا فلا يصح على ثبوتها عنه ، أفاده بعض المحققين.
Is there extravagance in wudu? He said: Yes, even if you are on a flowing river. And from it is wiping thrice with new water. And taqtir: making washing like wiping in it because it wastes the sunnah. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The best of matters are their middles.' And it is disliked to strike the face with water because it contradicts the nobility of the face; rather, he should pour it gently upon it. And it is disliked to speak like people because it distracts from supplications. And it is disliked to seek help from others, due to the saying of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): I saw the Messenger of Allah not fetching water for his wudu, so I hastened to fetch water for him. He said: 'O Umar, I do not like anyone to help me in my prayer without an excuse.' Because necessities permit prohibitions, so what of things without prohibition. And from Imam al-Wabari: there is no harm in it, for the servant used to pour for him. His saying: 'He said: Is there extravagance in wudu?' That which is in the narration of Ahmad, Abu Ya'la, al-Bayhaqi in Shu'ab, and Ibn Majah in his Sunan: 'Or in wudu?' with the addition of the conjunctive waw, implying an omitted phrase: 'Do you say this, and is there extravagance in wudu?' His saying: 'And taqtir' is not reaching the sunnah limit. If one limits to less than three, some say he sins, some say not, and some say he sins if habitual. Know that more than one have transmitted consensus that there is no fixed amount for wudu and ghusl water; rather it is according to sufficiency due to differences in people's natures. And from Aisha from the Messenger of Allah: in ghusl from janabah one sa' (eight pounds), and in wudu two pounds. That is two mudds; the mudd is a quarter of a sa'. The sunnah is thus. His saying: 'He makes washing like wiping' by making washing approach the state of oil, but it must drip, even if two drops, so that it is washing; otherwise, the wudu is not valid at all. His saying: 'And it is disliked to strike the face' as a tanzih (dislike of lesser degree), and similarly other limbs as in al-Dur. His saying: 'Because it contradicts the nobility of the face' and because it splashes used water, so avoiding it is better. He should not close his eyes nor tightly shut his mouth such that the redness of the lips and the edges of the eyelids and the roots of the eyelashes are hidden, because it is obligatory to deliver water to that place; if a spot remains untouched by water, wudu is not valid as in al-Halabi. His saying: 'He pours it gently upon it' meaning he sends water onto the face from the top of the forehead gently, then rubs it. His saying: 'And it is disliked to speak the speech of people' unless there is a need that would be lost by leaving it, stated by Ibn Amir Haj. His saying: 'Because it distracts from supplications' and to purify wudu from worldly impurities, as it is a prelude to worship. Some gnostics mentioned that presence of mind in prayer follows presence in wudu, and its absence follows its absence. His saying: 'And it is disliked to seek help, etc.' What was previously mentioned about it is that there is no harm in it. As for the hadith of Umar, it is weak and does not outweigh other evidence that indicates its permissibility, according to some researchers.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a commentary on Maraqi al-Falah, detailing many rulings on disliked (makruh) actions in wudu. The author first addresses the question of extravagance (israf) in wudu, confirming that even on a flowing river, using excessive water is considered wasteful and is makruh. He then discusses taqtir, i.e., using too little water, such as merely moistening the limbs like wiping rather than washing, which violates the sunnah. The hadith 'The best of matters are their middles' is cited as a general principle. Then, specific gestures are discussed: striking the face with water is makruh because it degrades the face's dignity; the proper way is to pour gently. Speaking worldly talk during wudu is also disliked because it distracts from recommended supplications. A more nuanced point is about seeking help from another person for wudu. The text narrates a hadith where Umar sought to help the Prophet but was told not to seek help in prayer without an excuse. However, the commentary notes that this hadith is weak and that other evidence suggests it is permissible, especially when there is a genuine need. Imam al-Wabari is quoted as saying there is no problem, as the servant would pour water for the Prophet. The commentary also clarifies that the amount of water for wudu and ghusl is not fixed; rather it is based on what suffices. The specific measurements from Aisha (two pounds for wudu) are sunnah but not obligatory. The text also warns that wudu becomes invalid if parts of the face (like the edges of lips or eyelids) remain dry.

Key Terms
الإسراف (al-israf): Using more water than necessary, even if on a flowing river.
التقتير (al-taqtir): Using too little water, less than the sunnah amount, e.g., only wiping without water dripping.
الإستعانة بغيره (al-isti'anah bi-ghayrih): Seeking help from another person to pour water or assist in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is sunnah
D) It is wajib

Key Takeaway
Moderation is key in wudu; israf and taqtir are both disliked, and seeking help is permissible when needed but better avoided without excuse.

Classification of Wudu: Fard and Wajib in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the divisions of wudu into fard and wajib with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fard and wajib wudu according to the Hanafi school, and state the practical consequences of each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, fard is from qat'i evidence and invalidates the act if omitted; wajib is from zanni evidence and requires compensation.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the Hanafi classification of wudu into distinct levels of obligation. Understanding the fard-wajib distinction is essential because it determines whether an act is valid when the obligation is missed, or merely requires compensation. The text applies this framework to prayer, touching the Quran, and tawaf, highlighting the school's unique approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of wudu
First, note that wudu is divided into three types: fard, wajib, and (implicitly) sunnah. The passage focuses on the first two. Recognize that the classification depends on the type of evidence and the consequence of omission.
Sunnah wudu is not discussed here but is implied as the third division.
2
Define fard and wajib terminologically
The text defines 'fard' as that which is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence, and its omission invalidates the act. 'Wajib' is established by zanni (speculative) evidence, and its omission requires compensation (dam) but does not invalidate the act. Understand this core distinction.
This is a hallmark of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguishing it from other schools that equate fard and wajib.
3
Apply the definition to specific cases
For prayer (including funeral prayer and prostration of recitation), wudu is fard because the evidence is qat'i. For tawaf, wudu is wajib because the evidence (hadith comparing tawaf to prayer with an exception) is zanni. Similarly, touching the Quran is fard based on the clear verse.
The text also discusses the ruling for touching the Quran's margins and translations.
4
Explore the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: if a person prays without wudu, the prayer is invalid (batil). If a person performs tawaf without wudu, the tawaf is valid but a dam (blood sacrifice) must be offered. This difference affects how one should act and what compensation is owed.
Contrast with Shafi'i school: for them, tawaf without wudu is invalid, requiring repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أوصاف الوضوء وقد ذكرها بعد بيان سببه وشرطه وحكمه وركنه فقال: (الوضوء على ثلاثة أقسام الأول منها أنه (فرض) كما قدمناه بدليله والمراد بالفرض هنا الثابت بالقطعي وأما المحدود، والمقدار فهو ما يفوت الجواز بفوته ليشمل الفرض الاجتهادي كربع الرأس، ونزلت آيته بالمدينة، وقد فرض بمكة (على المحدث) إذا أراد القيام (للصلاة) كما أمر الله تعالى ( ولو كانت الصلاة (نفلا) لأنّ الله لا يقبل صلاة من غير طهور كما تقدم وهو بفتح الطاء، وقال بعضهم : الأجود ضمه (و) كذا الصلاة الجنازة) لأنها صلاة وإن لم تكن كاملة (و) مثلها (سجدة التلاوة و كذا الوضوء فرض (لمس القرآن ولو آية مكتوبة على درهم أو حائط لقوله تعالى: (لا يمسه إلا المطهرون) وسواء الكتابة والبياض، وقال بعض مشايخنا : إنما يكره للحدث مس الوضع المكتوب دون الحواشي لأنه لم يمس حقيقة والصحيح إن مسها كمس المكتوب، ولو بالفارسية يحرم مسه اتفاقاً على الصحيح (و) القسم (الثاني) وضوء (واجب) وهو الوضوء للطواف بالكعبة) لقوله عليه السلام : الطواف حول الكعبة مثل الصلاة إلا أنكم تتكلمون فيه فمن تكلم فيه فلا يتكلمن إلا بخير ولما لم يكن صلاة حقيقة لم تتوقف صحته على الطهارة فيجب بتركه دم في الواجب وبدنة
Chapter on the descriptions of wudu. He mentioned them after explaining its cause, condition, ruling, and pillar. He said: Wudu is of three divisions. The first of them is that it is fard, as we have preceded with its proof. The meaning of fard here is that which is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence. As for the defined and measured, it is that which by its omission, permissibility is lost; this includes ijtihadi fard like a quarter of the head. Its verse was revealed in Medina, and it was made obligatory in Mecca upon the one in a state of minor ritual impurity when he intends to stand for prayer, as Allah commanded. Even if the prayer is supererogatory, because Allah does not accept prayer without purification, as has preceded. The word is with fatha on the ta, and some say the best is with damma. And likewise the funeral prayer, because it is prayer even if not complete. And similar to it is the prostration of recitation. And also wudu is fard for touching the Quran, even a verse written on a coin or wall, due to His saying: 'None touch it except the purified.' Both the script and the blank parts. Some of our shaykhs said: It is only disliked for the one in minor impurity to touch the written part, not the margins, because he does not truly touch it. The correct view is that touching the margins is like touching the written part. If it is in Persian, it is prohibited to touch it by agreement, according to the correct view. The second division is wajib wudu: it is wudu for tawaf around the Kaaba, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Tawaf around the Kaaba is like prayer, except that you speak in it, so whoever speaks must only speak good.' Since it is not truly prayer, its validity does not depend on purity. So if one leaves it, a dam (blood) is required for wajib tawaf, and a badana (camel) for obligatory tawaf.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a commentary on the classification of wudu in Maraqi al-Falah. The text states that wudu is divided into three types: fard, wajib, and (implicitly) sunnah. The lesson here focuses on the first two. First, wudu becomes fard (obligatory) when one intends to perform any prayer, including funeral prayer and prostration of recitation, and for touching the Quran. The author clarifies that 'fard' here means established by qat'i (definitive) evidence, as opposed to 'wajib' which is established by zanni (speculative) evidence. However, the term 'fard' is also used in a broader sense to include ijtihadi fard (like washing a quarter of the head) where the obligation is derived from ijtihad. The verse of wudu was revealed in Medina, but the obligation itself was already established in Mecca (when prayer was made obligatory). The text also addresses the ruling for touching the Quran: it is fard to have wudu for touching even a single verse, and the prohibition covers both the written text and the blank margins according to the correct view. If the Quran is written in Persian, it is still prohibited to touch. The second division is wajib wudu, exemplified by tawaf. The hadith compares tawaf to prayer, but unlike prayer, its validity does not require wudu. Therefore, performing tawaf without wudu is not invalid, but it is sinful and requires a compensation: a dam (sacrifice) for a wajib tawaf (like the farewell tawaf) and a badana for an obligatory tawaf (like the ziyarah tawaf) if one was in a state of janabah. This distinction between fard and wajib, where fard has qat'i proof and wajib has zanni proof, is a hallmark of the Hanafi school and affects the implications and compensations.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): An obligation established by qat'i (definitive) evidence; its omission invalidates the act.
الواجب (al-wajib): An obligation established by zanni (speculative) evidence; its omission requires compensation but does not invalidate the act.
الطواف (al-tawaf): Circumambulation of the Kaaba; requires wudu but not as a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the difference between fard and wajib wudu?
A) Fard is for prayer, wajib is for tawaf only
B) Fard is established by qat'i evidence and its omission invalidates the act; wajib by zanni evidence and requires compensation
C) Fard and wajib are synonyms
D) Fard requires niyyah, wajib does not

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes fard (qat'i evidence, invalidates act if omitted) from wajib (zanni evidence, requires compensation but act remains valid), a distinction with practical consequences for purification acts like wudu.

Wudu: The Obligatory (Fard) Division and Its Cases
Defining the first obligatory division of wudu with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the cases where wudu is fard according to the Hanafi school and identify the distinction between fard qat'i and fard ijtihadi.

Core Idea
Wudu is fard for prayer, janazah, sajdah, and touching the Quran; the measure of wiping (quarter head) is fard ijtihadi.

This lesson examines the first division of wudu: the obligatory (fard) category. The text clarifies that 'fard' here means obligation established by definitive evidence (qat'i), but it also includes obligations derived through interpretation (ijtihadi) like the specific measure of wiping. The passage then enumerates the acts for which wudu is required: all prayers (including supererogatory, funeral, and prostration of recitation), and touching the Quran, even its margins and Persian translations, based on the Quranic verse 'None touch it except the purified'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of wudu
The text states wudu is of three types. Identify the first as fard and note that the number does not restrict to only these three.
2
Distinguish between fard qat'i and fard ijtihadi
The author defines fard as that which is established by definitive evidence, but also includes the 'limited and measured' (al-miqdar) as a broader fard that includes ijtihadi obligations like wiping a quarter of the head.
3
List the cases where wudu is fard
From the passage: for prayer (even nafal), funeral prayer, sajdah tilawah, and touching the Quran (any part, including margins and Persian translations). Note the evidence for the Quran case.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on touching margins
Some scholars considered only the written part as makruh, but the correct view (sahih) is that margins are like the written text and require wudu.

Section on the descriptions of ablution. He said: Ablution is of three types. The first is that it is obligatory (fard) as we have mentioned with its proof. What is meant by fard here is that which is established by definitive evidence. As for the limited and measured, it is that which the validity is lost by its omission, to include ijtihadi fard like a quarter of the head. Its verse was revealed in Medina, and it was prescribed in Mecca. Upon the one in a state of minor impurity when he intends to stand for prayer as Allah commanded, even if the prayer is supererogatory, because Allah does not accept prayer without purification as previously mentioned. It is with fatha on ta, and some say the best is damm. Likewise, the funeral prayer, because it is a prayer even if not complete, and similar to it is the prostration of recitation. Likewise, ablution is obligatory for touching the Quran, even if one verse written on a dirham or a wall, due to His saying: 'None touch it except the purified.' The writing and the white space are the same. Some of our scholars said: It is only disliked for the one in impurity to touch the written part without the margins, because he has not touched it in reality. The correct view is that touching the margins is like touching the written part. If it is in Persian, it is forbidden to touch it by agreement on the correct view. Section: The Quran. In the descriptions of ablution. His saying: Ablution is of three types. The number does not indicate exclusivity, so it does not negate that it may be disliked, like ablution on ablution before changing the first sitting, or performing an act of worship that is not valid without it. And it may be forbidden, as if that is from waqf or school water. His saying: 'What is meant by fard here is that which is established by definitive evidence' – the intent is ablution in itself, disregarding its parts. His saying: 'The measured' is an explanatory conjunction. His saying: 'It is that which the validity is lost by its omission' – i.e., what is meant by fard with regard to it is the broader fard, which is that which the validity of the thing is lost if it is absent, so it includes the definitive with regard to the essence of washing and wiping, and the practical with regard to the measure. That is why the author said: to include etc. His saying: 'When he intends to stand' – i.e., begin, not the opposite of sitting, because what is meant by prayer includes supererogatory, and it is valid sitting. His saying: 'It is with fatha on ta' – tahur is the verbal noun and the thing with which one purifies oneself, or the pure purifier (Qamus). His saying: 'And similar to it is the prostration of recitation' – because they said: The same conditions are required for it as for prayer.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya defines the first division of wudu according to the Hanafi school. It begins by stating that wudu is of three types. The first type is 'fard' (obligatory). The commentator, al-Tahtawi, clarifies that 'fard' here means an obligation established by definitive evidence (qaṭʿī), as opposed to obligations based on interpretative reasoning (ijtihādī). This distinction is crucial: the obligation to wash the limbs is qaṭʿī, but the specific measure—like the obligatory quarter of the head for wiping—is ijtihādī. The obligation of wudu applies to anyone in a state of minor impurity (ḥadath) who wishes to pray, even supererogatory prayers. This is based on the Quranic command and the hadith that Allah does not accept prayer without purification. Wudu is also obligatory for the funeral prayer (ṣalāt al-janāzah) and for the prostration of recitation (sajdat al-tilāwah), as they are forms of prayer. Most importantly, wudu is obligatory for touching the Quran, even a single verse written on a coin or wall, based on the verse 'None touch it except the purified' (56:79). The commentator discusses that both the written text and the blank margins of a Quranic page are considered part of the Quran for this ruling, and that this applies even to a Persian translation. Some scholars held that only the written part is disliked to touch, but the correct view is that touching the margins is like touching the text. The number 'three' does not limit wudu to only these categories—it can also be disliked (e.g., repeating wudu without changing the place) or forbidden (e.g., using water from a waqf unlawfully).

Key Terms
الفرض الاجتهادي (al-fard al-ijtihādī): An obligation derived through interpretative reasoning (ijtihad), such as wiping a quarter of the head; its omission invalidates the act, but its evidence is not definitively certain.
المقدار (al-miqqādār): The specific quantity or measure of an obligatory act, e.g., the quarter of the head for wiping.
الطهور (al-ṭahūr): Purification; here it refers to the act of ablution or the means of purification (water).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a case where wudu is fard according to this passage?
A) Supererogatory prayer.
B) Funeral prayer.
C) Touching a dirham with a Quranic verse.
D) Reciting the Quran from a digital screen without touching the device.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is fard for any form of prayer and for touching the Quran, with the measure of wiping (quarter head) being an ijtihadi fard.

Wudu is Wajib for Tawaf: Ruling and Penalties
Wajib wudu for tawaf with explanation of proofs and penalties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that wudu is wajib for tawaf, explain the proof from the hadith, and apply the correct penalty (dam, badanah, sadaqah) for omitting wudu based on the type of tawaf and impurity.

Core Idea
Wudu is wajib for tawaf – valid without it but a penalty is owed.

In this passage, al-Tahtawi addresses the second division of wudu: the wudu that is wajib (obligatory but not fard) specifically for circumambulating the Kaaba. He cites the prophetic hadith that tawaf is 'like prayer' but with the exception of speaking, which indicates a partial resemblance. From this, the Hanafi school derives that purity is required but not a condition for validity. Al-Tahtawi then clarifies the graded penalties for omitting wudu depending on whether the tawaf is obligatory (farḍ), wajib (like farewell tawaf), or supererogatory, and whether the impurity is minor (ḥadath) or major (janābah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is performing wudu for tawaf. Determine its legal classification: fard or wajib? Here, it is stated as wājib – obligatory but not fard.
Distinguishing fard from wajib is a key Hanafi methodology: fard requires definitive proof and its omission invalidates the act; wajib is proven by probative evidence and its omission demands compensation but does not nullify the act.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the evidence cited: the hadith 'الطواف حول الكعبة مثل الصلاة إلا أنكم تتكلمون فيه' (Tawaf around the Kaaba is like prayer except that you speak in it). This is a sunnah aḥad, which is zannī al-thubūt (probable transmission).
The hadith is from Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah; its chain is not mutawātir, hence the ruling is wajib, not fard.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalālah (implication)
The hadith compares tawaf to prayer. In prayer, purity (tahārah) is a condition (shart) for validity; thus tawaf resembles prayer in requiring purity. However, the exception for speaking shows tawaf is not exactly prayer, so purity is not a condition but an obligatory element (wājib).
The partial resemblance (tashabbuh) leads to partial obligation: wudu is wajib but not a condition of validity.
4
Identify the consequence of omission
Since wudu is wajib but not a condition, omitting it results in a valid tawaf that is sinful (if deliberate) and requires compensation.
The penalty varies: for obligatory tawaf (farḍ) with minor impurity → dam; with major impurity → badanah. For wajib or supererogatory tawaf with minor impurity → sadaqah; with major impurity → dam.
5
Apply the distinctions
Practice classifying different scenarios: e.g., a pilgrim performs farewell tawaf (wajib) without wudu but is not in janābah. The penalty is sadaqah. Another performs tawaf al-ifāḍah (farḍ) in janābah → badanah.
The text explicitly outlines these categories. Memorizing the four cases solidifies the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فصل
القرآن
في أوصاف الوضوء. قوله : (ولما لم يكن صلاة حقيقة) يعن إنه لما أشبه الصلاة من وجه دون وجه قلنا : بوجوب الطهارة، وعدم توقف صحته عليها. قوله: (فيجب) بتركه دم في الواجب) اعلم أنه إذا طاف الفرض محدثاً وجب دم وإن كان جنباً فبدنه وإذا طاف الواجب كالوداع، أو النفل محدثاً فصدقة وجنباً فدم، فقوله: فيجب بتركه في الفرض للجنابة وصدقة في النفل بترك الوضوء كما ذكر في محله
And the second type: an obligatory (wajib) ablution, and it is ablution for circumambulation of the Kaaba, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Circumambulation around the Kaaba is like prayer, except that you speak in it; so whoever speaks in it should not speak except good.' And since it is not truly prayer, its validity does not depend on purity. So for its abandonment, a blood sacrifice is obligatory in the obligatory (tawaf) and a camel for janabah. Section: The Quran. In the descriptions of ablution. His saying: 'And since it is not truly prayer' – meaning, because it resembles prayer in one aspect but not in another, we said: Purity is obligatory for it, but its validity does not depend on it. His saying: 'So for its abandonment, a blood sacrifice is obligatory in the wajib' – know that if one performs an obligatory tawaf in a state of minor impurity, a blood sacrifice is owed; if in major impurity (janabah), then a camel. If one performs a wajib tawaf (like farewell tawaf) or a supererogatory tawaf in minor impurity, a charity is owed; if in janabah, a blood sacrifice. So his saying 'a blood sacrifice is owed' applies to the obligatory tawaf for janabah, and charity for supererogatory tawaf for abandoning ablution, as mentioned in its place.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage covers the second division of wudu: it is wajib (obligatory but not fard) for tawaf (circumambulation) of the Kaaba. The proof is the hadith that tawaf is like prayer except that speaking is allowed. Since tawaf resembles prayer in its purity requirement but differs in being valid without purity (though sinful), the Hanafi school classifies the obligation as 'wajib' rather than 'fard'. The consequence is that if one performs tawaf without wudu, the tawaf is valid but one must offer a penalty (dam or sadaqah) depending on the type of tawaf and the state of impurity. For obligatory tawaf (e.g., the tawaf al-ifadah in Hajj), if done without wudu, a dam (blood sacrifice) is owed; if done in janabah, a camel is owed. For wajib tawaf (like farewell tawaf) or supererogatory tawaf, the penalty is sadaqah for minor impurity and dam for janabah. This distinction highlights the Hanafi hierarchy of obligations: fard (requires full repetition if omitted), wajib (valid but requires compensation).

Key Terms
الطواف (al-ṭawāf): Circumambulation around the Kaaba, an act of worship in Hajj and Umrah.
دم (dam): Blood sacrifice (usually a sheep or goat) as a penalty for omitting a wajib in Hajj or Umrah.
بدنة (badanah): A camel or cow offered as sacrifice; the penalty for performing obligatory tawaf in a state of major impurity (janabah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person performs the obligatory tawaf (tawaf al-ifadah) without wudu, what is the penalty according to the Hanafi school?
A) The tawaf is invalid and must be repeated
B) A blood sacrifice (dam) is owed
C) Charity (sadaqah) is owed
D) No penalty, the tawaf is valid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that wudu for tawaf is wajib – valid without it but requiring compensation – based on the hadith comparing tawaf to prayer with an exception.

Wudu is Mandub in Various Situations
Listing the recommended circumstances for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the situations where wudu is mandub and distinguish the exceptional case of tafsir where wudu is required.

Core Idea
Touching tafsir without wudu is impermissible; other Islamic books are recommended to be touched with wudu.

Wudu is not only for obligatory prayers. In many situations, it is recommended (mandub) as an act of worship and veneration. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi enumerates several such situations, including touching Islamic books, sleeping in purity, renewing wudu, and after sins like backbiting. There is a special ruling for tafsir (Quran commentary) that elevates it beyond a mere recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of wudu being discussed
The author divides wudu into three types: obligatory, necessary (wajib), and recommended (mandub). This lesson covers the third type.
2
List the recommended situations
From the text: touching Islamic books (except tafsir), sleeping in a state of purity, waking up, renewing wudu for each prayer, performing wudu on wudu (if sitting place changes), and after speaking backbiting or lying.
Some situations have conditions, like changing sitting place for renewal.
3
Analyze the exception for tafsir
The text says 'except tafsir' and notes that this implies obligation. The commentator discusses whether it is fard or wajib. The cautious view is that wudu is required for touching any part of the Quran, including in tafsir books.
The commentary on al-Durar suggests obligation.
4
Understand the evidence (dalil)
The hadith of Bilal (rustling of sandals in Paradise) shows the virtue of constantly renewing wudu and praying after each event, which supports the recommendation of wudu for renewal and after minor impurities.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, so it recommends but does not obligate.
5
Recognize the nuances and exceptions
Backbiting and lying are haram, but wudu after them is recommended as a form of repentance. However, the exceptions where backbiting is permissible (e.g., complaint, warning) are detailed. Renewing wudu is wasteful if the sitting place has not changed.
This shows the detailed fiqh approach to recommend acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the third type: a recommended (mandub) ablution in many situations, like touching Islamic books, and it is permissible for the one in impurity to touch them except for tafsir (Quran commentary) – as in al-Durar. And this implies the obligation of ablution for touching tafsir, so it becomes part of the second type. And recommendation of ablution for sleeping in a state of purity, and also when waking from it – i.e., sleep – and renewing it for continuance on it due to the hadith of Bilal, may Allah be pleased with him. And for ablution on top of ablution if he changes his sitting place, because it is light upon light; if he does not change, it is wasteful. And it is restricted to ablution because washing on washing and tayammum on tayammum is frivolous. And after backbiting speech: mentioning your brother with what he dislikes in his absence, and lying: fabrication of what did not happen. All: i.e., the ablution in the wajib, blood is not complete, so reflect. His saying: 'like touching Islamic books' such as fiqh, hadith, and beliefs; so one should purify for them as a veneration. Al-Halwani said: We only attained this knowledge through veneration, for I never took a page except in a state of purity. Al-Sarakhsi had a stomach illness one night while repeating his lesson from his book, and he performed ablution that night seventeen times. End from the commentary. His saying: 'except tafsir' means it is not permissible (to touch without ablution) even if the tafsir is more, and it is true that it may be fard or wajib because the lack of permission accompanies both. So the author's saying 'it implies' requires reflection. 'Allamah Nuh transmitted from al-Jawharah and al-Siraj that books of tafsir: it is not permissible to touch the Quranic portion in them, and he may touch others unlike the Mushaf, because touching that is follow-up. His saying: 'for sleeping in a state of purity' – its apparent meaning is that one does not perform this recommended act unless sleep overtakes him while he is in purity. If he purifies, then lies down, then has an event and sleeps, he has not performed it. His saying: 'and when waking from it' is a motive for purification. His saying: 'The hadith of Bilal' – its meaning is that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, saw in a dream that he entered Paradise and Bilal was before him with the rustling of his sandals. He asked him about that, and he replied: Whenever I have an event, I perform ablution and pray two rak'ahs. Some eminent scholar was asked: Will sandals be worn in Paradise? He answered: Yes, using this hadith as evidence. His saying: 'If he changes his sitting place or performs with the first a specific act of worship due to the prescription of ablution.' His saying: 'and after backbiting speech' – there is no need to estimate a deleted noun, because backbiting is literally mentioning one's brother. And his saying 'by mentioning' depicts backbiting. And it is only called backbiting if it is truthful; if it is a lie, it is slander. Al-Khazin said: It is more severe than backbiting. And just as it is by speech, it is by everything that conveys the intended meaning. And just as it is forbidden to mention it with the tongue, it is forbidden in the heart and to listen to it. And it is permissible when complaining about an oppressor to someone who has the ability to give justice, and when seeking help to change evil and bring the sinner back to the right path. And when seeking a fatwa by saying to the mufti: So-and-so wronged me in such a way, or my husband does such-and-such. And when warning Muslims about evil by exposing the flaws of unreliable narrators and shaykhs. And like informing about a defect when consulting about marrying someone, or dealing with him, or traveling with him. And like informing about a defect in something one is buying without the seller knowing; in fact it is obligatory. And when mentioning a sinner with what he does openly, not with other things. And when identifying someone by a nickname he is known by, like al-A'mash, al-A'raj. And when showing compassion.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage details the third division of wudu: it is mandub (recommended) in many situations. The first set of situations includes touching Islamic books (fiqh, hadith, etc.) as an act of veneration, but tafsir (Quran commentary) is an exception: touching tafsir without wudu is not permissible, and some authorities consider it fard or wajib. The second set includes sleep: it is recommended to be in a state of wudu before sleeping, and to perform wudu upon waking. The third is renewing wudu for each prayer (or for continuance on it), based on the hadith of Bilal who said that whenever he had an event, he would renew his wudu and pray. The fourth is performing wudu on top of wudu, which is recommended if the sitting place has changed or if the previous wudu was used for a specific worship; otherwise it is wasteful. The fifth is after backbiting (ghībah) and lying: wudu is recommended to cleanse oneself from these sins. The commentary then discusses the exceptions where backbiting is permissible, such as complaining to a judge, seeking a fatwa, warning about a sinner's open sin, identifying a narrator's flaw, consulting about a marriage partner, and so on. This clarifies that while backbiting is generally haram, there are legitimate contexts where mentioning someone's fault is not considered backbiting.

Key Terms
الغيبة (al-ghībah): Backbiting: mentioning a person in their absence with what they dislike, if it is true.
الكذب (al-kadhib): Lying: fabricating something that did not happen; more severe than backbiting according to some scholars.
التفسير (al-tafsīr): Quran commentary; distinguished from the Mushaf and other Islamic books regarding the ruling of touching without wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding touching a book of tafsir (Quran commentary) without wudu?
A) Permissible and recommended
B) Makruh (disliked) but not haram
C) It is not permissible (requires wudu) and some consider it obligatory
D) The same as touching the Mushaf: fard to be in wudu

Key Takeaway
Wudu is recommended in many daily situations, but for touching tafsir, wudu becomes required to honor the Quranic verses it contains.

Mandub Wudu: Definition and Occasions
Defines mandub wudu and lists occasions with a refinement on wudu after wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the occasions of mandub wudu and distinguish them from wajib wudu and wasteful renewal.

Core Idea
Mandub wudu is a recommended act that becomes wasteful if done without changing location.

We have already covered obligatory and necessary wudu. This lesson focuses on the third category: mandub (recommended) wudu. The source text from al-Tahtawi clarifies when wudu is recommended and when it becomes wasteful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of wudu
Recognize that wudu can be fard (obligatory), wajib (necessary), or mandub (recommended). This passage deals with the third category.
The text mentions 'the third section' indicating the mandub category.
2
List the occasions for mandub wudu
From the passage, extract the specific situations: touching religious books (except tafsir), sleeping in a state of purity, waking from sleep, renewing wudu continuously (as per Bilal's practice), and wudu after wudu when changing place.
Note the exception for tafsir: some scholars deem it obligatory.
3
Understand the condition for renewing wudu
The text explicitly states that wudu after wudu is only recommended if one changes his sitting place. Without a change, it is considered wasteful (israf).
This is a critical refinement often misunderstood.
4
Relate to the hadith of Bilal
The hadith of Bilal (radiyallahu anhu) shows the virtue of constant wudu and prayer, providing the basis for the recommendation of continuous wudu.
The hadith is a proof for the desirability (mandub) of renewing wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة ۸۳ في الفرض للجنابة وصدقة في النفل بترك الوضوء كما ذكر في محله (و) القسم (الثالث) وضوء (مندوب) في أحوال كثيرة كمس الكتب الشرعية ورخص مسها للمحدث إلا التفسير كذا في الدرر وهو يقتضي وجوب الوضوء لمس التفسير فيكون مس القسم الثاني وندب الوضوء للنوم على طهارة و أيضاً إذا استيقظ منه) أي النوم (و) تجديده (للمداومة عليه لحديث بلال رضي الله عنه (وللوضوء على الوضوء) إذا تبدل مجلسه لأنه نور على نور وإذا لم يتبدل فهو إسراف
The Book of Purification 83: (It comes) in the obligation for major impurity and charity in the supererogatory by leaving wudu as mentioned in its place. (And) the third section: wudu (recommended) in many circumstances, such as touching religious books, and it is permitted for a minor impure person to touch them except for Tafsir – this is in al-Durar. And it implies the obligation of wudu for touching Tafsir, so it becomes the second section. And recommended wudu for sleeping in a state of purity, and also when waking from it (i.e., sleep), and renewing it (wudu) for continuity based on the hadith of Bilal (may Allah be pleased with him). And (wudu) after wudu when one changes his sitting place, for it is light upon light; but if he does not change place, it is wasteful.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah discusses the third category of wudu: mandub (recommended). The author clarifies that while wudu is obligatory for janaba and wajib in other cases, this section covers recommended occasions. The text lists: touching religious books (permitted for a person without wudu except for Quranic tafsir, which some deem obligatory as per al-Durar), sleeping while in a state of purity, waking from sleep, renewing wudu continuously following the practice of Bilal (radiyallahu anhu) as reported in a hadith, and performing wudu again after a previous wudu only when one changes location, otherwise it is wasteful (israf).

Key Terms
وضوء مندوب (wudu' mandub): The third category of wudu that is recommended (mandub) in specific situations, such as touching religious books, sleeping in purity, renewing wudu, etc.
الوضوء على الوضوء (al-wudu' 'ala al-wudu'): Performing wudu again after already having wudu; it is recommended only if one changes location, otherwise wasteful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is renewing wudu (al-wudu' 'ala al-wudu') recommended?
A) Without any condition
B) Only when one changes his sitting place
C) Only after sleeping
D) Only for touching religious books

Key Takeaway
Mandub wudu is recommended in specific circumstances; renewing wudu is only recommended when you change your sitting place.

Wudu after Sinful Speech: Ghiba, Kadhib, Namima
Explains wudu after sinful speech and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing wudu after ghiba, kadhib, and namima, and list the eight exceptions where ghiba is permitted.

Core Idea
Wudu after backbiting is recommended, but backbiting itself is haram unless within specific exceptions.

This lesson from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya explains the recommendation of wudu following sinful speech: backbiting (ghiba), lying (kadhib), and slander (namima). The passage defines these terms and details the exceptional circumstances where backbiting becomes permissible, such as seeking justice or warning others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ghiba (backbiting), which is defined as mentioning a fellow Muslim in his absence with something he dislikes, provided it is true. Its ruling is haram. The passage also recommends wudu after committing such sins.
If the statement is false, it is slander (buhtan) and even worse.
2
List the exceptions for ghiba
The text provides eight exceptions: complaining to someone who can stop oppression, seeking help to change evil, asking a mufti for a fatwa, warning Muslims about unreliable narrators, advising before marriage/business, reporting a defect in a purchase (obligatory), mentioning a sinner's open sin, and using known nicknames for identification. Sincere advice is also permitted.
These exceptions are based on necessity and public interest.
3
Define namima
Namima is tale-bearing: carrying speech from one group to another to cause corruption. It is strictly prohibited, but permitted in cases like war, reconciliation, or pleasing family.
Namima is distinct from ghiba; both are major sins.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a real-life situation, determine if wudu is recommended and if the speech falls under an exception. For example, if someone backbites out of spite, wudu is recommended but the backbiting remains sinful.
Wudu is a means of purification but does not absolve the sin unless repentance is made.

Al-Halwani said: We only attained this knowledge through veneration – I never took paper except with purity. And al-Sarakhsi had a stomach ache one night while reviewing his lesson, so he performed wudu that night seventeen times. End of explanation. His statement: (except Tafsir) i.e., it is not permitted even if Tafsir is more, and it is true that it could be fard or wajib because the lack of permission coexists with both. It is not called backbiting unless it is truthful; as for if it is false, it is slander. Al-Khazin said: It is more severe than backbiting. And just as it occurs by speech, it occurs by other means that convey the intended meaning. And just as mentioning it with the tongue is forbidden, it is forbidden with the heart and listening to it. His statement: (al-nammam al-mudrib) – Majd did not mention this meaning in al-Qamus; he rather said: al-namm is raising speech, spreading it and corrupting, and he mentioned other meanings.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya details the recommendation of wudu after sinful speech, specifically backbiting (ghiba), lying (kadhib), and slander (namima). The commentary extensively defines ghiba: it is mentioning a fellow Muslim in his absence with something he dislikes, provided it is true; if false, it is slander (buhtan) and even worse. The author lists exceptions where mentioning faults is permissible: complaining to someone who can stop oppression, seeking help to change evil, asking a mufti for a legal ruling in a marital dispute, warning Muslims about unreliable narrators or teachers, giving advice before marriage or business deals, informing a buyer of a defect unknown to him (which is actually obligatory), calling a sinner by his open sin, using nicknames like 'al-A'mash' for recognition, and giving sincere advice. However, namima (tale-bearing) is defined as carrying speech between people to cause corruption, and prohibition is lifted only for specific purposes like war or reconciliation.

Key Terms
غيبة (ghiba): Mentioning a fellow Muslim in his absence with something he dislikes, provided it is true; otherwise it is slander (buhtan).
نميمة (namima): Slandering by carrying speech from one group to another for the purpose of causing corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Performing wudu after sinful speech is a recommended act of devotion, but the sins themselves (ghiba, kadhib, namima) remain haram except in specific exceptional situations.

Mandub Wudu for Other Occasions
Final segment listing additional mandub wudu occasions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the remaining occasions for mandub wudu and explain the evidence for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Mandub wudu is recommended after sins, laughter, washing the dead, before prayer, and for janaba – each supported by sunna.

This passage completes the enumeration of occasions where wudu is recommended (mandub). While some occasions like before prayer are general, others like after washing a dead body are based on specific hadith. Understanding these helps the student see the scope of wudu beyond obligatory acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that the text lists occasions for mandub (recommended) wudu, not obligatory wudu.
These are additional to the obligatory causes of wudu.
2
List the occasions
Extract each occasion from the text: after every sin, after reciting bad poetry, after loud laughter outside prayer, after washing a dead person, after carrying a dead person, before each prayer, before ghusl janaba, and for a junub when eating, between intercourse, or before sleep.
3
Classify the evidence
For each occasion, identify whether the evidence is a specific hadith (e.g., washing dead) or a general principle (e.g., wudu expiates sins).
The hadith for washing/carrying the dead uses imperative form, indicating recommendation in Hanafi usul.
4
Distinguish the ruling level
Confirm that all these are mandub, not fard or wajib. The Hanafi school interprets the imperative in the hadith as recommendation unless there is evidence for obligation.
The dalil block explains the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) بعد (كل خطيئة وإنشاد (شعر) قبيح لأن الوضوء يكفر الذنوب الصغائر (وقهقهة خارج الصلاة) لأنها حدث صورة وغسل ميت وحمله لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «من غسل ميتاً فليغتسل» حمله فليتوضأ ولوقت كل صلاة لأنه أكمل لشأنها وقبل غسل الجنابة) لورود السنة قوله: (وللجنب عند إرادة أكل الخ) أما الوضوء بين الجماعين، وعند النوم، فالمراد به الشرعي في قول أبي حنيفة ومالك والشافعي وأحمد والجمهور كما في شرح البخاري للبدر
And after every sin and recitation of bad poetry, because wudu expiates minor sins. And loud laughter outside prayer, because it is a form of hadath (ritual impurity). And washing a dead person and carrying him, based on his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever washes a dead person, let him perform ghusl' (and whoever carries him, let him perform wudu). And for every prayer, because it completes its excellence. And before ghusl of janaba, because the sunna has come. His statement: (and for the junub when desiring to eat etc.) – as for wudu between two acts of intercourse and at the time of sleep, the intended meaning is the legal wudu according to Abu Hanifa, Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad, and the majority, as in Sharh al-Bukhari by al-Badr.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This final segment lists additional occasions for optional wudu: after every sin (since wudu expiates minor sins), after reciting vulgar poetry, after loud laughter outside prayer (which is a ritual impurity requiring wudu for removal), after washing a deceased person and after carrying him (based on a hadith: the Prophet said whoever washes a dead body should perform ghusl, and whoever carries it should perform wudu), before each prayer to enhance its merit, before ghusl for janaba due to prophetic sunna, and for a junub person before eating or between two acts of intercourse or before sleep (the latter according to the majority of scholars including Abu Hanifa).

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud laughter outside of prayer; it is considered a hadath (ritual impurity) requiring wudu for its removal.
غسل ميت (ghusl al-mayyit): Washing the deceased; the hadith recommends ghusl for the washer and wudu for the carrier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Wudu is recommended after several specific acts including washing a dead body, and the imperative in the hadith is interpreted as recommendation in Hanafi fiqh.

The Recommendation of Wudu for a Junub
Commentary on the recommended acts of wudu for a junub and their evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu for a junub before eating, sleeping, and similar acts, identify the hadith evidence, and explain why it is recommended rather than obligatory.

Core Idea
Wudu for a junub is recommended (sunnah), not obligatory — the imperative in the hadith indicates nadb in Hanafi usul.

The state of janabah requires ghusl for major impurity, but the Hanafi school also encourages wudu before certain actions. This lesson explains which acts are recommended for a junub and the evidence from the Sunnah, clarifying the disagreement within the school and with other madhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage discusses wudu for a junub before eating, drinking, sleeping, resuming intercourse, anger, and other acts. The ruling is recommended (sunnah muakkadah), not obligatory.
The ruling is based on hadith evidence that is zanni al-dalala.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'Whoever washes a deceased person, let him perform ghusl; whoever carries him, let him perform wudu.' Additionally, other ahadith specifically recommend wudu for a junub.
The quoted hadith is used analogically for the junub's wudu.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'فليغتسل' and 'فليتوضأ' indicates a command. In Hanafi usul, a bare imperative indicates obligation unless there are qara'in (indicators) showing recommendation. Here, other evidences (e.g., lack of blame for omission) shift it to nadb.
This is the key hermeneutical move distinguishing fard from sunnah.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that the recommendation for wudu before resuming intercourse is abrogated. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) maintain the recommendation. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
Al-Tahtawi sides with the majority.
5
Compare with other schools
Imam Ahmad considers wudu obligatory for carrying the deceased; the Hanafi school holds it recommended. This is a practical difference in the level of requirement.
The Hanafi view is based on the principle that commands can be interpreted as nadb when there are opposing indicators.

Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi expands on the rulings of wudu and ghusl. The author explains that certain acts like backbiting (namimah) are not permissible except in specific contexts like war or reconciliation. Then he discusses the recommendation of wudu after sins, laughter in prayer, washing a deceased person, and carrying him, citing the hadith 'Whoever washes a deceased person, let him perform ghusl; whoever carries him, let him perform wudu.' The main focus shifts to actions recommended for a person in a state of major impurity (junub). Before eating, drinking, sleeping, resuming intercourse, when angry, before reading Quran or hadith, studying knowledge, calling adhan, giving sermons, visiting the Prophet's mosque, and standing at Arafah, it is recommended to perform wudu. This is to exit the disagreement of other scholars like Imam Ahmad who consider wudu obligatory for some acts. The author also mentions that touching a woman or one's own private part with the palm of the hand does not invalidate wudu according to the preferred view, ensuring worship is valid by consensus.

Key Terms
الجنب (al-junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
الوضوء (al-wudu'): Washing of specific body parts before prayer; here, recommended for a junub before eating, sleeping, etc.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Full body washing to remove major impurity (janabah).
النميمة (an-namimah): Slander or backbiting by transmitting speech to cause corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (sunnah)
C) Permissible (mubah) without recommendation
D) Forbidden (haram)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, wudu for a junub before eating, sleeping, resuming intercourse, and similar acts is a recommended sunnah, not an obligation, due to the nature of the evidence and usul principles.

Recommended Acts for a Junub
Lists recommended acts for junub with proofs and scholarly differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended acts for a junub and identify the evidence and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf from the Hanafi perspective.

Core Idea
A junub is encouraged to perform wudu or wash hands before many acts; it is recommended, not obligatory.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi lists numerous actions for which a person in major impurity (junub) is recommended to perform wudu or at least wash hands and mouth. The author cites prophetic hadith as proofs and notes an internal Hanafi disagreement regarding wudu before repeating intercourse. Understanding these recommendations helps one follow the Sunnah with proper reverence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Level
Note that all listed acts are classified as mustahabb (recommended, sunnah ghayr muakkadah) in the Hanafi school. This means doing them is rewarded but omitting them is not sinful.
Compare with fard or wajib which are obligatory.
2
List the Recommended Acts
Go through the passage and enumerate each action for which wudu or washing is recommended for the junub: eating, drinking, sleeping, repeating intercourse, anger, reading Quran, reading hadith, studying knowledge, adhan, iqama, khutba, visiting Prophet's mosque, standing at Arafat, sa'i, and after eating camel meat.
Some acts have specific conditions, e.g., anger is to calm it.
3
Examine the Dalil (Proof)
The author provides hadith evidence for key recommendations. For example, the hadith from Aisha about sleeping while junub, and the hadith about wudu before repeating intercourse. Analyze why these indicate recommendation, not obligation, using Hanafi usul principles.
The hadith are ahad (single) and not mutawatir, so they yield zanni dalalah.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that wudu between intercourse is not recommended, interpreting the relevant hadith as indicating mere permissibility. The majority (including Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold it is recommended. Understand the basis of each view.
The author sides with the majority and rejects Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is the recommendation, as per the majority of Hanafis. This is based on the apparent meaning of the hadith and the principle that the Sunnah of the Prophet is to be followed in these acts.
The tarjih authority is the author of the Hashiya and general Hanafi practice.

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya discusses the recommended acts for a person in the state of major impurity (junub). The author lists various actions for which it is recommended for a junub to perform wudu or at least wash hands and mouth. These include: eating, drinking, sleeping, repeating intercourse, anger (to calm it), reading Quran, reading hadith and its narration (out of respect), studying sacred knowledge, adhan, iqama, khutba (marriage sermon), visiting the Prophet's mosque, standing at Arafah, performing sa'i between Safa and Marwah, and after eating camel meat (due to the opinion of Imam Ahmad that it necessitates wudu, to exit disagreement). The author then provides supporting narrations from the Prophet ﷺ. A hadith from Aisha shows that the Prophet would wash his private parts and perform wudu for prayer before sleeping while junub. Another hadith recommends wudu before repeating intercourse. The Hanafi position is that these are recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory. Abu Yusuf differs regarding wudu between intercourse, considering it not recommended. The author also explains that wudu before eating is understood linguistically (washing hands and mouth) and narrates that the Prophet would wash his hands before eating while junub. The overall goal is to perform acts of worship with full reverence and to follow the Sunnah, avoiding disagreement with other schools.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl (full body wash) due to sexual intercourse or seminal emission.
وضوء (wudu): Ritual washing of hands, mouth, nose, face, arms, wiping head, and washing feet performed before prayer and other acts.
مستحب (mustahabb): A recommended act; doing it brings reward but omitting it does not incur sin.
