Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Nullifiers of Wudu: Definition and First Nullifier
Defines naqd linguistically and technically, then states the first nullifier: anything exiting from the two passages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naqd linguistically and technically, and state the first nullifier of wudu in the Hanafi school, including its scope and the exception for rabh al-qabl.

Core Idea
Any substance exiting the two passages nullifies wudu, even if unusual, except for a twitch (rabh al-qabl).

This lesson begins the study of what nullifies wudu (nawaqid al-wudu). First, we understand the term 'naqd' itself—how its meaning shifts from physical demolition to legal nullification. Then we tackle the very first nullifier: anything that exits from the two passages. The ruling is broader than you might expect, but also has a clear exception.

Guided Path
1
Define 'naqd' linguistically and technically
Identify the two meanings: when applied to physical objects (like a wall) it means demolition of structure; when applied to wudu it means removal of the legal state that permits prayer.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the legal concept—the nullifier is the substance itself, not the act of exiting.
2
State the first nullifier and its scope
The first nullifier is 'anything that exits from the two passages (front and back), even if little.' This includes both usual (urine, feces) and unusual (worm, stone) substances.
'Sabeel' (passage) is used because both openings are paths for exiting matter.
3
Recognize the exception: rabh al-qabl
A twitch or vibration (rabh) of the male or female organ does not nullify wudu. The text clarifies it is not wind, and even if it were, no filth is involved from the front.
This exception is the 'most correct opinion' (al-asahh), indicating a considered position within the school.
4
Distinguish between wind from back vs. front
Wind from the back nullifies because it passes over filth in the anus. Wind from the front does not, as the front passage is not inherently impure. The wind itself is pure and does not make clothes impure.
This distinction avoids the misconception that all wind nullifies.
5
Apply the precautionary ruling for a fistulated woman (mufdah)
For a woman whose urinary and fecal passages have mixed, her wind nullifies as a precaution (ihtiyatan) due to the possibility of impurity. If the mixing is between urine and intercourse passages only, wind from the front does not nullify.
Additional rulings: she cannot be made halal for a thrice-divorced husband without pregnancy; intercourse in the front is prohibited unless possible without transgression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الضد
هو
بمعنى فاصل، أو مفصول أو ذو فصل مبتدأ أو خبر قوله : (هو طائفة من المسائل) أي مطلقاً وتقييده في الشرح بالفقهية لخصوص المقام، وزاد غيره مترجمة بكتاب، ولا باب. قوله : (النقض الخ) فهو حقيقة في الأول مجاز في الثاني بجامع الأبطال، وقيل مشترك. قاله السيد، وأصله للإتقاني قوله : (من إقامة المطلوب (بها والمطلوب من الوضوء إستباحة الصلاة، ونحوها قوله: (منها ما خرج من (السبيلين أفاد أن الناقض الخارج لا خروجه لأن المؤثر في رفع ضده، وإنما الخروج علة لتحقق الوصف الذي هو النجاسة لذات الخارج، وشرط عمل الضد في ضده، لا إنه هو العامل لأنه لا يوصف بطهارة، ولا نجاسة لأنه معنى من المعاني، وإضافة النقض إليه إضافة إلى علة العلة والأولى إضافة الحكم إلى نفس العلة. قوله : (وإن كان ريحاً لا نجاسة فيه الأولى أن يقول، وإن كان ريحاً فليس منبعثاً عن نجاسة لأنه يفيد بمفهومه أن . الدبر نجسة وليس كذلك كما أفاده بعد ويحتمل أن المراد لا ريح نجاسة فيه أي في القبل يمر عليها ريحه حتى يكون ناقضاً وهو الذي يفيده كلامه بعد قوله : (فلا ينجس مبتل الشياب) والإستنجاء منه بدعة . قوله: (فينقض ريح المفضاة إحتياطاً) الأولى الواو، والمراد بها من إختلط مسلك ،بولها وغائطها بخلاف من إختلط مسلك بولها ووطئها فلا نقض بالريح الخارج من أمامها على الصحيح، وتختص الأولى بحكمين آخرين، أحدهما أنها لا تحل لمن طلقها ثلاثاً بوطء الثاني ما لم تحبل لإحتمال الوطء في الدبر، والثاني حرمة جماعها إلا أن يمكنه الوطء في القبل بلا تعد وفي الهندية عن المحيط عد من النواقض سقوطه من أعلى اهـ قال بعض الفضلاء، ولعله لعدم خلوّه عن خروج خارج غالباً، وهو لا يشعر، والخنثى غير المشكل فرجه الآخر كالجرح، وهو المعول عليه، والمشكل ينتقض، وضوءه بمجرد الظهور من كل .
It is a group of rulings whose rules have changed relative to what was before it. Nullification of wudu: al-naqd, when added to physical objects like the demolition of a wall, means the demolition of its structure; when added to abstract concepts like wudu, it means taking it out from fulfilling what is required of it. Al-nawaqidh is the plural of naqidah. Twelve things: among them is what exits from the two passages, even if little. The front and back are called 'sabeel' because it is the path for exit. Whether usual or unusual like a worm or a stone, except for the 'rabh al-qabl' (vibration of the front) of the male and female, in the most correct opinion, because it is a twitch not wind, and even if it were wind, there is no filth in it. Wind from the back is a nullifier because it passes over filth, since its essence is pure, so it does not make wet clothes impure according to the general scholars. So the wind of a fistulated woman (with an opening) nullifies as a precaution. Exit is verified by the appearance of moisture. The opposite: it is by the meaning of separater, or separated, or having separation, whether mubtada or khabar. His statement: (It is a group of rulings) i.e., absolutely, but the sharh restricts it to jurisprudential rulings due to the context. Others added: translated with 'kitab' and not 'bab'. His statement: (al-naqd etc.) So it is literal in the first and metaphorical in the second due to the common factor of annulment, and it is said to be polysemous. This is said by al-Sayyid, and its origin is from al-Itqani. His statement: (from fulfilling what is required of it) and what is required from wudu is the permissibility of prayer and the like. His statement: (among them is what comes out of the two passages) indicates that the nullifier is the external substance, not its exit, because the effecting factor in raising its opposite. Rather, exit is the cause for the realization of the attribute which is impurity of the external substance, and a condition for the action of the opposite upon its opposite, not that it is the agent itself, because it is not described with purity or impurity, as it is a conceptual meaning. Adding nullification to it is adding to the cause of the cause; rather it is better to add the ruling to the very cause. His statement: (And if it were wind with no filth in it) it would be better to say: and if it were wind not emanating from filth, because by its implication it indicates that the anus is impure, which is not the case as he later indicated. It is probable that he means: there is no wind of filth in it, i.e., in the front it passes over its wind so that it becomes a nullifier, and that is what his later speech indicates. His statement: (so it does not make wet clothes impure) and istinja from it is an innovation. His statement: (so the wind of a fistulated woman nullifies as a precaution) the first is with a waw (and). By it is meant one whose passage for urine and feces has mixed; unlike one whose passage for urine and intercourse have mixed, so wind exiting from her front does not nullify according to the correct view. The former is distinguished by two other rulings: one, she is not permissible for one who divorced her thrice by intercourse of the second husband unless she becomes pregnant, due to the possibility of intercourse in the back; second, the prohibition of having intercourse with her unless he can have intercourse in the front without overstepping. In al-Hindiyyah from al-Muhit: counted among the nullifiers is falling from a height. End quote. Some scholars said: Perhaps because he is rarely free from something exiting externally, and he is unaware. The hermaphrodite whose other organ is not problematic is like a wound, and this is the relied-upon view. The problematic one: his wudu is nullified merely by the appearance from each.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the nullifiers of wudu (nawaqid al-wudu) in the Hashiya. First, the author defines the term 'naqd' linguistically and technically. Linguistically, when applied to physical objects like a wall, it means demolition. In legal context, applied to wudu, it means removing the ability to perform acts that require wudu, like prayer. Then, the author states that the nullifiers are twelve things, but only the first is detailed here: anything that exits from the two passages (front and back), even in small quantity. This includes both usual and unusual matter like worms or stones. However, an exception is made for 'rabh al-qabl' (vibration or twitching) of the male or female organ, as it is merely a twitch, not actual wind. Even if it were wind, it would not be from filth. Wind from the back nullifies because it passes over impure matter, but the wind itself is pure, so it does not make wet clothes impure. For precaution, wind from a fistulated woman (with an abnormal passage) nullifies. The exit is confirmed by the appearance of moisture. The commentary then parses the earlier statements, emphasizing that the nullifier is the physical substance that exits, not the act of exiting. The author clarifies that wind from the back is a nullifier because it passes over filth, but the essence of wind is pure. For a woman with a fistulated passage (mixed urine and feces tracts), precaution dictates that her wind nullifies wudu. Conversely, if the mixing is between urine and intercourse passages, wind from the front does not nullify. Additional rulings for such a woman are given: she cannot be made permissible for her thrice-divorced husband by a second husband's intercourse unless she becomes pregnant (due to possibility of anal intercourse), and it is forbidden to have intercourse with her unless she can be entered from the front without transgression. Finally, falling from a height is counted as a nullifier, and the ruling for a hermaphrodite with unclear private parts is explained.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Nullification; linguistically demolition, legally the removal of the state of purity that enables acts like prayer.
السبيلين (al-sabeelayn): The two passages (front and back) of the human body; literally means 'the two roads'.
مفضاة (mufdah): A woman with a fistulated passage where the urinary and fecal tracts have mixed, creating an abnormal exit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What nullifies wudu according to the first nullifier discussed in this lesson?
A) The act of exiting from the two passages
B) Any substance that exits from the two passages, even if unusual
C) Only urine and feces
D) Wind from the front regardless of origin

Key Takeaway
The first nullifier of wudu is any substance exiting the two passages, but a mere twitch (rabh al-qabl) does not nullify.

Definition of Naqd and the First Nullifier: Exit from the Two Passages
Defines naqd and lists the first nullifier of wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of naqd (nullification) and identify the first nullifier of wudu, applying the exception for wind from the front.

Core Idea
The nullifier is what exits the two passages, but wind from the front is excepted because it carries no impurity.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational definition of naqd (nullification) and the first of the twelve nullifiers of wudu. The author clarifies that nullification is the removal of the state of purity, not a physical breaking. The key ruling is that anything exiting the two passages—front or rear—nullifies wudu, with one important exception: wind from the front does not nullify because it is merely a twitch or, if wind, it passes over no impurity. This lesson also refines the understanding by explaining the role of exit as a cause and the precautionary ruling for a muftāh woman.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term naqd
Identify that naqd literally means demolition (as of a wall) and metaphorically means nullification of an abstract state (like wudu). The common factor is abrogation of the previous effect.
This distinguishes literal and metaphorical usage.
2
Identify the first nullifier
The passage states: 'what exits from the two passages, even if small.' This includes all substances (urine, feces, worms, stones) regardless of normalcy.
The nullifier is the substance that exits, not the act of exiting.
3
Apply the exception and refinements
Wind from the front does not nullify because it is either a twitch or, if wind, carries no impurity. Wind from the rear nullifies because it passes over impurity. For a muftāh woman (fused urinary and fecal passages), her wind nullifies out of precaution.
The exit must be actual (moisture). The commentary refines the matn's wording.

رفع ضده، وإنما الخروج علة لتحقق الوصف الذي هو النجاسة لذات الخارج، وشرط عمل الضد في ضده، لا إنه هو العامل لأنه لا يوصف بطهارة، ولا نجاسة لأنه معنى من المعاني، وإضافة النقض إليه إضافة إلى علة العلة والأولى إضافة الحكم إلى نفس العلة. قوله : (وإن كان ريحاً لا نجاسة فيه الأولى أن يقول، وإن كان ريحاً فليس منبعثاً عن نجاسة لأنه يفيد بمفهومه أن الدبر نجسة وليس كذلك كما أفاده بعد ويحتمل أن المراد لا ريح نجاسة فيه أي في القبل يمر عليها ريحه حتى يكون ناقضاً وهو الذي يفيده كلامه بعد قوله : (فلا ينجس مبتل الشياب) والإستنجاء منه بدعة . قوله: (فينقض ريح المفضاة إحتياطاً) الأولى الواو، والمراد بها من إختلط مسلك بولها وغائطها بخلاف من اختلط مسلك بولها ووطئها فلا نقض بالريح الخارج من أمامها على الصحيح، وتختص الأولى بحكمين آخرين، أحدهما أنها لا تحل لمن طلقها ثلاثاً بوطء الثاني ما لم تحبل لإحتمال الوطء في الدبر، والثاني حرمة جماعها إلا أن يمكنه الوطء في القبل بلا تعد وفي الهندية عن المحيط عد من النواقض سقوطه من أعلى اهـ قال بعض الفضلاء، ولعله لعدم خلوّه عن خروج خارج غالباً، وهو لا يشعر، والخنثى غير المشكل فرجه الآخر كالجرح، وهو المعول عليه، والمشكل ينتقض، وضوءه بمجرد الظهور من كل .
The Book of Purity: It is a collection of rulings whose legal status has changed relative to what came before. Naqd (nullification), when attributed to physical objects like the demolition of a wall, means the dissolution of their structure; when attributed to abstract meanings like wudu, it means removing them from the ability to achieve the intended purpose. Al-Nawaqid (nullifiers) is the plural of naqida. (Twelve things) among them (what exits from the two passages, even if small: the front and rear are called 'passages' because they are a path for what exits, and it is the same whether normal or otherwise, like a worm or a stone) except wind from the front, the male and female private parts, according to the most correct view, because it is a twitch, not wind; and if it is wind, there is no impurity in it. Wind from the rear is a nullifier because it passes over impurity, for its essence is pure, so it does not make wet garments impure according to the general scholars. So the wind of a woman with a fused passage (muftāh) nullifies out of precaution. Exit is realized by the appearance of moisture. Al-Didd (opposite) means something separating. His saying: 'It is a set of rulings' meaning absolutely, and its restriction in the commentary to fiqh is due to the context. Others added 'translated as book, not chapter'. His saying: 'al-naqd' etc., so it is literal in the first and metaphorical in the second due to the common factor of annulment; some say it is homonymous. This is said by al-Sayyid, and its origin is from al-Itqani. His saying: 'from establishing the intended' — the intended of wudu is to permit prayer and similar acts. His saying: 'among them is what exits from the two passages' indicates that the nullifier is the thing that exits, not the act of exiting, because the effective factor is the removal of its opposite. Rather, the exit is the cause for the realization of the description of impurity inherent in the substance of what exits, and it is a condition for the action of the opposite upon its opposite, not that it is the active factor itself, because the exit cannot be described as pure or impure, being a abstract meaning. Attributing naqd to it is attributing to the cause of the cause; it would be better to attribute the ruling to the cause itself. His saying: 'even if it is wind with no impurity in it' — it would be better to say, 'even if it is wind, it does not originate from impurity,' because the implication is that the rear is impure, which is not correct as he later clarifies. It is also possible that the meaning is that there is no impure wind in the front that its wind would pass over so as to be a nullifier, which is what his later statement indicates. His saying: 'so it does not make wet garments impure' — and performing istinja from it is an innovation. His saying: 'so the wind of a muftāh woman nullifies as a precaution' — the waw (and) is more appropriate. The muftāh is one whose passage for urine and feces has fused; unlike one whose passage for urine and intercourse has fused, in which case wind exiting from her front does not nullify according to the correct view. The former has two other rulings: first, she is not lawful for one who has divorced her thrice by intercourse with a second husband until she becomes pregnant due to the possibility of intercourse in the rear; second, intercourse with her is forbidden unless he can enter from the front without transgression. In al-Hindiya from al-Muhit, 'falling from above' is counted among the nullifiers. Some scholars said: perhaps because it is rarely free from some exit, and he is unaware. The khuntha (hermaphrodite) whose other private part is not ambiguous is like a wound, and that is relied upon. The ambiguous one: his wudu is nullified simply by appearance from any [opening].
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling section from the hashiya. It begins by defining the term 'naqd' (nullification) as it applies to wudu: it is the removal of the state of purity, rendering it incapable of fulfilling its purpose (prayer). The author then lists the first of the twelve nullifiers: anything that exits from the two passages (front and rear), regardless of quantity or type (e.g., urine, feces, worms, stones). An exception is made for wind from the front (i.e., from the male or female private parts) because it is merely a twitch without wind, or if wind, it carries no impurity. Wind from the rear is a nullifier because it passes over the impure area. The commentary clarifies that the nullifying factor is the actual substance exiting, not the act of exiting itself. The case of a woman with a fused passage (muftāh) is discussed: if her urinary and fecal passages are fused, wind from her rear nullifies wudu out of precaution; if her urinary and reproductive passages are fused, wind from the front does not nullify. Also, the khuntha (hermaphrodite) is discussed: if one private part is dominant (unambiguous), the other is treated like a wound; if ambiguous, anything exiting from either part nullifies wudu. The commentary refines the matn by explaining nuances and intra-school positions.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): Nullification of wudu, i.e., removal of the state of ritual purity.
السبيلين (al-sabilayn): The two passages: the front (urinary) and the rear (anal).
المفضاة (al-mufḍāh): A woman whose urinary and fecal passages have fused into one opening.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The first nullifier of wudu is anything exiting the two passages, but wind from the front is excepted because it carries no impurity.

Nullification by Birth Without Blood: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on postpartum purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the ruling for a woman who gives birth without blood, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Birth without blood nullifies wudu, but ghusl is only precautionary; the mufta bihi is wudu alone.

This passage addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding postpartum purification. When a woman gives birth but observes no blood, does she need wudu or ghusl? The Sahibayn rule that she only needs wudu because nifas is defined by blood, while Abu Hanifa requires ghusl as a precaution. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance textual definitions with practical realities.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi baseline
Identify the agreed ruling: birth (even without blood) nullifies wudu. This is from the zahir al-riwayah.
The nullification itself is not disputed.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
Note the point of dispute: whether ghusl is also required in addition to wudu.
Abu Hanifa demands ghusl; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not.
3
Trace the Sahibayn's reasoning
Understand their dalil: nifas is defined by the presence of blood (al-nifās yata'allaqu bi al-dam). Since no blood is seen, she is not nufasā', so only wudu is needed for the moisture.
They rely on the literal absence of blood.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's precaution
Understand his basis: childbirth is rarely free from even a trace of blood (ghalaba al-ghalaba). Therefore, ghusl is precautionary (ihtiyat).
This is an example of istihsan based on common occurrence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the authoritative position: the Sahibayn's view (wudu only) is considered 'al-sahih' (the correct one) in the later school. Al-Sadr al-Shahid gave fatwa with Abu Hanifa, but the text affirms the Sahibayn as the sounder view.
This illustrates the Hanafi hierarchy: later scholars may prefer one view over another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينقضه ولادة من غير رؤية دم ولا تكون نفساء في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد آخراً وهو الصحيح لتعلق النفاس بالدم ولم يوجد وعليها الوضوء للرطوبة وقال أبو حنيفة : عليها الغسل احتياطاً لعدم خلوه عن قليل دم ظاهراً وصححه في الفتاوى وبه أفتى الصدر الشهيد رحمه الله
And birth without seeing blood nullifies wudu, and she does not become a nufasā' (woman in postpartum bleeding) according to the later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and this is the correct view, because postpartum bleeding is connected to blood, which is absent. She must perform wudu due to the moisture. Abu Hanifa said: she must perform ghusl out of precaution because her state is rarely free from a small amount of blood apparently. This was validated in al-Fatawa, and al-Sadr al-Shahid (may Allah have mercy on him) gave fatwa accordingly.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a woman who gives birth but does not see any blood. The issue is whether this nullifies wudu and whether she is considered in a state of postpartum bleeding (nifas). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), she does not become nufasā' because nifas is defined by the presence of blood. Therefore, she only needs to renew her wudu due to the moisture associated with birth. This is considered the sounder view (al-sahih). Abu Hanifa, however, holds that she must perform ghusl (full ablution) out of precaution, because it is rare for childbirth to be completely free of any blood. This view was preferred in some fatwa collections, and al-Sadr al-Shahid issued fatwa accordingly. The dispute hinges on whether the default assumption (ghalaba) of blood presence overrides the absence of visible blood. The text clarifies that the operative ruling in fatwa (mufta bihi) is the view of the Sahibayn, but Abu Hanifa's view is also considered. This is a typical Hanafi ikhtilaf where the majority (Sahibayn) rely on the literal absence of blood, while Abu Hanifa applies istihsan (juristic preference) based on the common occurrence.

Key Terms
النفاس (al-nifās): Postpartum bleeding; a woman in nifas is required to perform ghusl after it ends.
الرطوبة (al-ruṭūba): Moisture discharged during childbirth; requires renewal of wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must perform ghusl because childbirth inherently requires it.
B) She only needs to perform wudu due to the moisture.
C) She needs neither wudu nor ghusl.
D) She must perform both wudu and ghusl.

Key Takeaway
Birth without blood nullifies wudu but does not require ghusl according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Nullification by Flowing Impurity: The Hanafi Position and Its Proof
Ruling with proof on nullification by flowing impurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that any flowing impurity from the body nullifies wudu, identify the hadith proof, and apply the definition of 'flow' to distinguish between nullifying and non-nullifying cases.

Core Idea
Any flowing impurity from the body nullifies wudu — even if not from the two passages.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that wudu is nullified by any liquid impurity (such as blood or pus) that flows from any part of the body, not just the two passages. The proof is the hadith 'Wudu is required from every flowing blood,' attributed to numerous Companions. We will unpack the definition of 'flow' and the exceptions made for hardship, and explore an intra-madhhab difference concerning blister fluid.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling and Its Proof
Identify what nullifies wudu according to this passage: any flowing impurity from other than the two passages. The proof is the hadith 'al-wudu min kulli dam sa'il'.
The ruling is based on a hadith transmitted by ten Companions and others.
2
Define 'Flow' (Sayalan)
For the two passages, flow means appearance at the opening. For other body parts, flow means the impurity reaches a place that requires purification (i.e., where washing or wiping is required).
Internal movement (e.g., inside the eye) does not count as flow due to hardship.
3
Identify the Exceptions
Blood that flows inside the eye or inside a wound does not nullify because purification of those areas is excused due to hardship. However, blood that reaches the nasal cavity or ear canal does nullify because those areas are to be washed/wiped.
The exception is based on istihsan (juristic preference) for ease.
4
Analyze the Ikhtilaf on Blister Water
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: some scholars (e.g., al-Hasan al-Basri, al-Halwani) state that clear blister water does not nullify, providing ease for those with skin conditions. The relied-upon view is that it does nullify if due to illness.
Al-Tahtawi notes both views but considers the nullifying view as al-sahih.
5
Understand the Hanafi Use of Istihsan
The Hanafi school applies istihsan to relax the ruling in cases of hardship (e.g., internal flow), while maintaining the general principle from the hadith.
Istihsan here overrides the apparent generality of the hadith for internal flow.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقض الوضوء نجاسة سائلة من غيرهما أي السبيلين لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الوضوء من كل دم سائل وهو مذهب العشرة المبشرين بالجنة وابن مسعود وابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت وأبي موسى الأشعري وغيرهم من كبار الصحابة وصدور التابعين كالحسن البصري وابن سيرين رضي الله عنهم والسيلان في السبيلين بالظهور على رأسهما وفي غير السبيلين بتجاوز النجاسة إلى محل يطلب تطهيره ولو ندبا فلا ينقض دم سال في داخل العين إلى جانب آخر منها بخلاف ما صلب من الأنف وقوله كدم وقيح إشارة إلى أن ماء الصديد ناقض كماء الثدي والسرة قوله : (وفي غير السبيلين بتجاوز النجاسة إلى محل الخ) والمراد أن تتجاوزه ولو بالعصر، وما شأنه أن يتجاوز لولا المانع. كما لو مصت علقة، فامتلأت بحيث لو شقت لسال منها الدم كذا في الحلبي . قوله : (إلى) محل أعم من العضو، والثوب، والمكان. قوله يطلب تطهيره بالغسل، أو المسح فينتظم الموضع الذي سقط عنه حكم التطهير بعذر قاله ابن الكمال. قوله : (ولو ندباً فإذا نزل الدم إلى قصبة الأنف نقض صرح به في المعراج وغيره لأنّ المبالغة بإيصال الماء إليها في الإستنشاق لغير الصائم مسنونة، وفي البدائع إذا نزل الدم إلى صماخ الأذن يكون حدثاً اهـ، وليس ذلك إلا لكونه يندب مسحه في الوضوء، ويجب غسله في الغسل . قوله : فلا ينقض دم سال في داخل العين الخ) وكذا ما سال في باطن الجرح إلى الجانب الآخر، وحقيقة التطهير فيهما ممكنة، وإنما سقط حكمه للحرج. قوله : (كماء الثدي والسرة الخ قال في البحر: الجرح، والنفطة، وماء السرة، والثدي والأذن والعين إذا كان لعلة سواء في الأصح أي في النقض والظاهر أن القيد راجع إلى الأربعة الأخيرة. وعن الحسن أن ماء النفطة لا ينقض قال الحلواني : وفيه توسعة لمن به جرب أو جدري، أو مجل بالجيم، وهو ما يكون بين الجلد، واللحم، وفي الجوهرة عن الينابيع الماء الصافي إذا خرج من النفطة لا ينقض. وفي المغرب هي بفتح النون، وكسر الفاء وزن كلمة الجدري، وبكسر النون وسكون الفاء القرحة التي امتلأت، وحان قشرها
And impurity that flows from elsewhere, i.e., other than the two passages, nullifies wudu, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Wudu is required from every flowing blood' — and this is the madhhab of the ten Companions given glad tidings of Paradise, and Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and others among the senior Companions and leading Tabi'un such as al-Hasan al-Basri and Ibn Sirin, may Allah be pleased with them. 'Flow' in the two passages is realized by appearance at their head; in other than the two passages, it is realized by the impurity's reaching a place whose purification is required, even if merely recommended. So blood that flows inside the eye to another part does not nullify, unlike that which flows from the nose. His saying, 'like blood and pus' indicates that pus-like water is a nullifier, like water from the breast and navel. His saying: 'In other than the two passages, by the impurity's reaching a place whose purification is required' — meaning it reaches it even by squeezing, and anything that would reach it except for a barrier. For example, if a leech sucks blood and becomes filled so that if cut, blood would flow from it — similar in al-Halabi. His saying: 'to a place' — general, including limb, garment, or location. His saying: 'whose purification is required by washing or wiping' — thus includes places where purification ruling has lapsed due to an excuse, as said by Ibn al-Kamal. His saying: 'even if merely recommended' — so if blood reaches the nasal cavity, it nullifies, as stated in al-Mi'raj and others, because exaggerating by reaching water there in snuffing during wudu is sunnah for a non-fasting person. In al-Bada'i': if blood reaches the hollow of the ear, it becomes a hadath (impurity). This is only because it is recommended to wipe it in wudu and obligatory to wash it in ghusl. His saying: 'So blood that flows inside the eye does not nullify' — similarly, what flows inside a wound to the other side. The reality of purification in both is possible; its ruling is dropped due to hardship. His saying: 'like breast water and navel water' — al-Bahr says: a wound, blister, navel water, breast, ear, and eye if due to an illness are equal in the correct view, i.e., in nullification. It appears that the qualification refers to the last four. Al-Hasan reported that blister water does not nullify. Al-Halwani said: This gives leeway for one with scabies, smallpox, or 'mijl' (something between skin and flesh). In al-Jawhara from al-Yanabi', clear water exiting from a blister does not nullify. In al-Mughrib, 'nafṭah' is pronounced with fathah on the nun and kasrah on the fa, same weight as 'jadari', or with kasrah on the nun and sukun on the fa, meaning a sore that has filled and is about to peel.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage states that any liquid impurity (like blood, pus, or fluid from a wound, breast, or navel) that flows from anywhere on the body other than the two private parts nullifies wudu. The proof is the hadith 'Wudu is required from every flowing blood,' which is attributed to ten Companions and others. The commentary defines what constitutes 'flow' (sayalan): for the two passages, it means the impurity appears at the opening; for other parts, it means the impurity reaches a place that requires purification (e.g., the skin, but not including areas where purification is excused). Specifically, blood flowing inside the eye to another part does not nullify because of hardship, but blood that reaches the nasal cavity or ear canal does nullify because those areas are required to be washed or wiped (even if only recommended). The commentary also addresses fluids from blisters: some scholars say clear water from a blister does not nullify to provide ease for those with skin conditions. The relied-upon view is that liquid from a wound, blister, breast, navel, ear, or eye (if due to illness) nullifies wudu, but there is a minority view for ease. This reflects the Hanafi use of istihsan (juristic preference) to relax rulings in cases of hardship.

Key Terms
السيلان (al-sayalān): Flow: for the two passages, appearance at the opening; for elsewhere, reaching a place requiring purification.
النفطة (al-nafṭah): A blister or sore that has filled with fluid and is about to peel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does blood that flows from a wound inside the mouth (e.g., a tooth extraction) nullify wudu if it does not exit the mouth?
A) Yes, because all blood nullifies wudu.
B) No, because it did not reach a place that requires purification on the exterior.
C) Only if it is swallowed.
D) Yes, because the mouth is like the two passages.

Key Takeaway
Flowing impurity from anywhere on the body nullifies wudu, but internal flow (e.g., inside eye/wound) is excepted due to hardship.

Nullification by Blood and Childbirth: Rulings and an Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on two nullifiers of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on nullification of wudu by blood from the passages and by childbirth without visible blood, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn regarding the latter.

Core Idea
Blood from the passages nullifies wudu; childbirth without blood requires only wudu according to the relied-upon view.

In this lesson, we examine two related nullifiers of wudu: (1) the appearance of blood at the exit, even reaching the foreskin, and (2) childbirth without visible blood. The first ruling is straightforward and based on a hadith. The second involves a well-known intra-Hanafi disagreement between the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and his two disciples (Sahibayn). Understanding the positions and the reasoning behind the tarjih is essential for proper fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two nullifiers
The passage states that blood from the passages nullifies wudu, and that childbirth without seeing blood also nullifies wudu (according to the matn). Note the explicit wording.
2
Understand the basis for the blood ruling
The hadith 'al-wudu' min kulli dam sa'il' is the primary dalil. The imperative form indicates obligation.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on childbirth
Compare the views: Sahibayn hold that nifas is tied to blood, so without blood she is not nafsa and only needs wudu for moisture; Abu Hanifa holds that ghusl is precautionary due to the overwhelming presence of blood.
The root cause is whether the general presence of blood can be treated as certainty.
4
Identify the tarjih
The text declares Sahibayn's view as 'al-sahih' and notes that al-Sadr al-Shahid issued fatwa accordingly. This is the mufta bihi.
Tarjih is based on the principle that nifas is defined by actual blood.
5
Note the related rule for the deceased
The tanbih clarifies that the nullification applies to the living; for a dead person after ghusl, any emission is simply washed without repeating ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دم) على رأس المخرج ولو إلى القلفة على الصحيح ( وينقضه) أي الوضوء (ولادة من غير رؤية ولا تكون نفساء في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد آخراً وهو الصحيح لتعلق النفاس بالدم ولم يوجد وعليها الوضوء للرطوبة وقال أبو حنيفة : عليها الغسل احتياطاً لعدم خلوه عن قليل دم ظاهراً وصححه في الفتاوى وبه أفتى الصدر الشهيد رحمه الله

قوله : (ولو إلى القلفة بفتحات وبوزن غرفة وهي ما يقطع في الختان. قوله: (لعدم خلوه) أي المولود المعلوم من المقام، أو حال الولادة. قوله : (ظاهراً) أي في الظاهر أي أن الغالب أن لا يخلو النفاس عنه، فنزل الغالب منزلة المتحقق .

تنبيه : ما سال من السبيلين إنما يعد ناقضاً لطهارة الحي أما الخارج من الميت بعد تغسيله، فيغسل، ولا يعاد الغسل
Blood) on the head of the exit even if to the foreskin (qulfah) according to the correct view. And it nullifies (i.e., wudu) childbirth without seeing blood, and she is not considered in nifas according to the later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is the correct view because nifas is connected to blood and it was not found. She must perform wudu for moisture. Abu Hanifa said: she must perform ghusl as a precaution because the birth cannot be free of a little blood apparently. And he (Abu Hanifa) was corrected in the Fatawa and by al-Sadr al-Shahid. His statement: (even if to the foreskin) with fatha and on the measure of ghurfa, and it is what is cut in circumcision. His statement: (due to its lack of being free) i.e., the known offspring from the context, or the state of birth. His statement: (apparently) i.e., outwardly, meaning that the majority that nifas is not free from it, so the prevailing is treated as certain. Note: What flows from the two passages is considered a nullifier of the purification of the living, but what exits from the dead after washing it is washed and ghusl is not repeated.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about two rulings related to nullifiers of wudu. First, the matn states that blood appearing at the head of the male organ (even reaching the foreskin) nullifies wudu. The foreskin (qulfah) is the part cut in circumcision. Second, the passage addresses childbirth without visible blood. The later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) is that the woman is not in a state of nifas (postpartum bleeding) because nifas is defined by the presence of blood. Since no blood was seen, she is not a nafsa; however, due to moisture (rutubah) from the birth, she must renew wudu. Abu Hanifa held that she must perform ghusl (full bath) as a precaution, because birth is rarely completely free of blood, so the general rule (ghalabah) is treated as certain. The text notes that Abu Hanifa's view was corrected in later fatawa and adopted by al-Sadr al-Shahid, but the preferred position (mufta bihi) according to the author appears to be that of Sahibayn, as stated by the phrase "wa huwa al-sahih". The hashiya explains the definitions and the reasoning: the general prevalence (ghalabah) is treated like certainty (mutahaqqiq). Additionally, a tanbih (note) clarifies that flowing from the two passages nullifies wudu for a living person, but for a dead person after ghusl, any emission is simply washed off without repeating the ghusl.

Key Terms
قلفة (qulfah): The foreskin of the male organ, the part cut in circumcision.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding; a woman in nifas is exempt from salah and fasting.
ولد (walad): The newborn child; the context of childbirth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, if a woman gives birth without seeing any blood, what is required?
A) Ghusl and wudu
B) Only wudu
C) Only ghusl
D) Nothing is required

Key Takeaway
Blood from the passages nullifies wudu; childbirth without blood nullifies wudu and requires only wudu according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Nullification by Flowing Impurities and Vomit
Ruling and conditions for nullification of wudu by impurities from other than the two passages and by vomit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for nullification of wudu by flowing impurities from other than the two passages and by vomit, and apply the Hanafi conditions of flow (sailan) and exceeding (tajawuz) to specific cases.

Core Idea
A flowing impurity nullifies wudu only if it exceeds to a place requiring purification; vomit always nullifies regardless of change.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi expands on the ruling that wudu is nullified by impure fluids leaving the body. The central proof is the hadith 'al-wudu' min kulli dam sa'il' which generalizes flowing blood to all flowing impurities. The commentary clarifies the precise condition of 'flowing' and 'exceeding' and distinguishes various cases such as eye discharge, blister fluid, and vomit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Proof and Its Scope
Recognize the hadith 'al-wudu' min kulli dam sa'il' and note that it is used as evidence for nullification by any flowing impurity, not just blood.
The hadith is ahad but accepted by the Hanafi school; its generality is extended via qiyas.
2
Distinguish Conditions for Non-Passage Impurities
Understand that for impurities from other than the two exits, nullification requires both flowing (sailan) and exceeding (tajawuz) to a place that is washed or wiped in wudu, even if only recommended.
Flow from the two passages is defined by appearance at the exit; for other areas, it means spreading beyond the source.
3
Apply Conditions to Specific Cases
Examine examples: blood inside the eye does not nullify because it does not reach an external washing area; nasal discharge nullifies if it reaches the nasal cavity (recommended to wash). Blister water: clear fluid does not nullify per the preferred view, but pus mixed with blood does.
The distinction hinges on whether the fluid is pure (clear) or impure (mixed with blood/pus) and whether it exceeds the source.
4
Analyze Chronic Discharge and Doubt
Learn the ruling for a person with a chronic eye condition: the order to perform wudu for each prayer is recommended (istihbab), not obligatory, unless there is strong probability (ghalabat al-ẓann) that the discharge is pus or matter.
Certainty is not removed by doubt; the default is no nullification.
5
State the Ruling on Vomit and Note the Dispute
Vomit of food, water, or blood nullifies wudu regardless of change. A minority view (Hasan al-Basri) says immediate vomiting does not nullify, but the mufta bihi is nullification in all cases.
The dispute centers on whether the contents have become impure through digestion; the Hanafi position is that they are impure and nullify.

والتحريك لغة فيها ذكره العلامة نوح وفي التبيين، ولو كان بعينه رمداً، أو عمش يسيل منها الدموع. قالوا: يؤمر بالوضوء لوقت كل صلاة لإحتمال أن يكون صديداً أو قيحاً قال العلامة الشلبي في حاشيته عليه قال وإن تجاوز إلى محل يلحقه حكم التطهير إذا كان ماءً صافياً أما غير الصافي بأن كان مخلوطاً بدم، أو قيح، أو صديد فإنه ناقض إذا وجد السيلان بأن تجاوز العصابة، وإلا لم ينقض ما دامت الورقة في موضع الكي معصبة أو قيحاً لولا الرباط لسال في غالب ظنه انتقض وضوءه في الحال لا قبل ذلك لكون النجاسة انفصلت عن موضعها أما قبل حلها فالنجاسة في موضعها لم تنفصل ولو لم يمكن قطع السيلان حقيقة أو حكماً كقطعه بالربط فهو معذور وإلا لا حتى لو كان لا يمتنع العذر بالربط أو الحشو وجب ذلك نقله السيد قوله : (وإن لم يتغير) أشار به إلى أنه لا فرق بين أنواع القيء سواء قاء من ساعته أم لا وقال الحسن : إذا تناول طعاماً أو ماء ثم قاء من ساعته لا ينتقض وضوءه لأنه طاهر حيث لم يستحل والذي اتصل به قليل قيء فلا يكون حدثاً فلا يكون نجساً وكذا الصبي إذا ارتضع وقاء من ساعته لا يكون نجساً والصحيح أنه حدث ونجس في الكل كما في الحلبي قيل وقول الحسن هو المختار
And wudu is nullified by a flowing impurity from other than the two passages, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Wudu is from every flowing blood.' This is the school of the ten, and Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zaid ibn Thabit, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and other senior Companions and leading Tabi'in like al-Hasan al-Basri and Ibn Sirin. And flowing (sailan) for the two passages is by appearing on their heads, but for other than the two passages, it is by the impurity exceeding to a place that requires purification, even if only recommended. So blood flowing inside the eye to another side does not nullify, unlike what dries from the nose. And his statement 'like blood and pus' indicates that pus water is a nullifier, like water of the breast and navel. His statement: (and for other than the two passages, by the impurity exceeding to a place, etc.) meaning that it exceeds even if by squeezing, and what would normally exceed were it not for an obstacle, as if a leech sucks and fills so that if it were cut, blood would flow. His statement: (to) a place more general than the limb, garment, or location. His statement: 'that requires purification' by washing or wiping, so it includes a place that has fallen from the ruling of purification due to an excuse, as said by Ibn al-Kamal. His statement: (even if only recommended) so if blood goes down to the nasal cavity, it nullifies, as stated in al-Mi'raj and others, because exaggerating in reaching water to it in istinshaq for a non-fasting person is sunnah. In al-Bada'i', if blood goes to the ear canal, it is a hadath, and that is only because wiping it is recommended in wudu and washing it is obligatory in ghusl. His statement: (so blood flowing inside the eye does not nullify) likewise what flows inside a wound to the other side, and purification in both is possible in reality, but its ruling is dropped due to hardship. His statement: (like water of the breast and navel) al-Bahr said: the wound, blister, water of the navel, breast, ear, and eye if due to an illness are the same in the correct view regarding nullification, and apparently the condition refers to the last four. And from al-Hasan that blister water does not nullify. Al-Halwani said: there is leeway in it for one with mange, smallpox, or a majl (a swelling between skin and flesh). In al-Jawharah from al-Yanabi', clear water if it comes out of a blister does not nullify. And al-Mughrib: it is with fatha of nun and kasr of fa' on the measure of jadari, and with kasr of nun and sukun of fa' the sore that has filled and its peel is near. And the ear if due to illness, on the correct view. And it nullifies vomit of food or water even if unchanged, or of blood. And the movement is a language for it mentioned by Allamah Nuh. In al-Tabyin, if one has ophthalmia (ramad) or a chronic eye infection from which tears flow, they are commanded to perform wudu at the time of every prayer because it may be pus or matter. Al-Allamah al-Shalabi said in his hashiah: Al-Shaykh Kamal al-Din said in the chapter on mustahadah: I say this reasoning indicates that it is a recommended order, for doubt and possibility that it is a nullifier does not necessitate ruling of nullification, since certainty is not removed by doubt. But if it is known that it is pus or matter through overwhelming probability from doctors or a sign that overcomes the afflicted person's assumption, it becomes obligatory. In al-Munyah, it is narrated from Muhammad: the shaykh said: if one has ophthalmia and tears flow from it, I order him to perform wudu at the time of every prayer because I fear that what flows from it may be pus, so he is a person with an excuse. The commentator on it narrated from al-Kamal what al-Shalabi narrated, then said: evidence for this being a recommended order is in Sharh al-Zahidi after this issue, and from Hisham in his Jami': if it is pus, then she is like a mustahadah, otherwise like a healthy woman. As for their statement: 'water of a wound, blister, navel, breast, eye, and ear if due to illness are the same', it should be understood as when what comes out of the eye is changed due to that illness. In al-Fath from al-Tajnis: al-gharab in the eye if water flows from it, it nullifies because it is like a wound and not tears; it is with tashid a swelling in the canthus. And in al-Durr it is with fatha then sukun: it is a vein in the eye that waters and does not stop. I say: does the same difference of opinion apply to clear tears of the eye as to blister water? Apparently yes, as there is no difference. Al-Arif billah Sayyidi Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said: one should rule according to the narration of no nullification by clear water that comes out of a blister in the cauterization of a chickpea, and that what comes out of it does not nullify even if it exceeds to a place subject to purification if it is clear water. As for non-clear water mixed with blood, pus, or matter, it is a nullifier if flowing occurs, i.e., exceeding the bandage. Otherwise, it does not nullify as long as the paper at the cauterization site is bandaged with the bandage, even if it fills with blood or pus, unless it flows around the bandage or blood or pus comes through. As for it appearing without exceeding the bandage, that is from the wound itself and is not a nullifier. If he removes the bandage and takes out the paper and cloth and finds blood or pus that would have flowed were it not for the bandage, in his overwhelming assumption, his wudu is nullified immediately, not before, because the impurity became separated from its place. Before removal, the impurity was in its place and not separated. If the flow cannot be stopped in reality or legally (like by tying it), then he is excused; otherwise not, so that if the excuse is not prevented by tying or stuffing, then it becomes obligatory. This was transmitted by al-Sayyid. His statement: (even if it does not change) indicates that there is no difference between types of vomit, whether he vomits immediately or not. Al-Hasan said: if he consumes food or water and then vomits it immediately, his wudu is not nullified because it is pure as it has not been digested, and a small amount of vomit connected to him is not an event and not impure. Similarly, if an infant breastfeeds and vomits immediately, it is not impure. The correct view is that it is an event and impure in all cases, as in al-Halabi. It was said that al-Hasan's view is the chosen one.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the ruling that wudu is nullified by flowing impurities from body parts other than the two passages (urethra and anus). The primary proof is the hadith: 'Wudu is from every flowing blood,' which is attributed to the ten Companions promised Paradise and many other Companions and Tabi'in. The condition for nullification is that the impurity flows and exceeds (tajawuz) to a place that requires purification, even if that purification is only recommended (nadb). The hashiya clarifies various aspects: the impurity must truly exceed its place, even if it would flow were it not for an obstacle (like a bandage); the place of purification includes limbs, garments, or locations; if the impurity reaches an area that is recommended to be washed (like the nasal cavity in istinshaq for a non-fasting person), it nullifies. However, blood or impurities flowing inside a cavity like the eye or inside a wound that does not reach an external washing area does not nullify, due to hardship (haraj). The text then discusses specific cases: water from a blister (naptah), breast, navel, ear, and eye differ regarding nullification if the discharge is clear (mā' ṣāf) versus mixed with blood or pus. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) for clear water from a blister is that it does not nullify, based on a narration from Hasan al-Basri. The passage also covers the case of chronic eye conditions (ramad, 'amash) where tears may be uncertain; in such cases, the order to perform wudu for each prayer is considered recommended (istihbāb) rather than obligatory, but becomes obligatory if there is strong probability (ghalabat al-ẓann) that the discharge is pus or matter. The last part addresses vomit: vomit of food, water, or blood nullifies wudu, regardless of whether it changes from its original state. There is a dispute (attributed to Hasan al-Basri) that if someone vomits immediately after consuming without digestion, it does not nullify. The text states that the correct view is that it is both an event (ḥadath) and impure (najis) in all cases, though it notes that some prefer al-Hasan's view.

Key Terms
نجاسة سائلة (najāsah sā'ilah): Flowing impurity from a body part other than the two passages, which nullifies wudu if it exceeds to a place requiring purification.
سيلان (sailān): Flowing; for impurities from the two passages it means appearing at the exit; for other body parts it means exceeding the immediate site.
تجاوز (tajāwuz): Exceeding; the impurity moves from its source to a place that normally requires purification (washing or wiping).
قيء (qay'): Vomit; the involuntary or voluntary ejection of stomach contents through the mouth. Nullifies wudu regardless of type (food, water, blood, bile).
نفطة (nafṭah): A blister or pustule; fluid-filled swelling under the skin. Its water (mā' al-nafṭah) is subject to dispute regarding nullification.
ماء الصديد (mā' al-ṣadīd): Pus or matter; a thick yellowish fluid from infection. It nullifies wudu because it is considered a flowing impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does blood from a wound (not from the two passages) nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) It nullifies as soon as it appears, regardless of quantity.
B) It nullifies only if it flows to a place that requires purification, even if only recommended.
C) It nullifies only if it is in a liquid state.
D) It nullifies only if it is accompanied by pus.

Key Takeaway
A flowing impurity from other than the two passages nullifies wudu only if it exceeds to a place that is washed or wiped; clear blister fluid does not nullify; vomit always nullifies.

Taharah — Purification

Ear and Eye Discharge: When Does It Nullify Wudu?
Commentary on nullifiers of wudu: ear and eye discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ear and eye discharge as nullifiers of wudu and distinguish between cases where renewal is obligatory versus recommendatory.

Core Idea
Doubt about impurity does not nullify certainty; only confirmed pus or flowing impurity breaks wudu.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a subtle question: does fluid from the ear or eye nullify wudu? The answer depends on whether the discharge is ordinary tears or pus due to illness, and whether it flows. The commentary distinguishes between recommendatory and obligatory rulings based on certainty and probability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Ear discharge due to illness, and eye discharge from ophthalmia. The base text states ear discharge nullifies wudu; eye discharge triggers a command to renew wudu.
Focus on the distinction between ear and eye.
2
Analyze the reasoning for ear discharge
Why does ear discharge nullify wudu? Because it is considered impure (najis) due to illness, by default. The commentary affirms this as the correct view (as-sahih).
The basis is qiyas on other flowing impurities.
3
Examine the ruling for eye discharge
For eye discharge, scholars said one is commanded to perform wudu for each prayer time out of fear it may be pus. Al-Tahtawi explains this command is recommendatory (istihbab), not obligatory, because doubt does not remove certainty.
This distinguishes recommendatory from obligatory.
4
Identify when it becomes obligatory
If the discharge is known to be pus or matter through overwhelming probability (e.g., physician's report), then wudu becomes obligatory. Otherwise, it is simply recommended.
Certainty overrides doubt.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
Al-Tahtawi extends the discussion to other wounds, blisters, and the like. Clear water from a blister does not nullify; if mixed with blood, it nullifies if it flows beyond the bandage.
The key is flow and impurity content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والأذن إذا كان لمرض على الصحيح (و) ينقضه (قيء طعام أو ماء) وإن لم يتغير (أو علق). والتحريك لغة فيها ذكره العلامة نوح وفي التبيين، ولو كان بعينه رمداً، أو عمش يسيل منها الدموع. قالوا: يؤمر بالوضوء لوقت كل صلاة لإحتمال أن يكون صديداً أو قيحاً قال العلامة الشلبي في حاشيته عليه قال الشيخ كمال الدين في فصل المستحاضة: وأقول هذا التعليل يقتضي أنه أمر استحباب فإن الشك والإحتمال في كونه ناقضاً لا يوجب الحكم بالنقض إذ اليقين لا يزول بالشك والله تعالى أعلم نعم إذا علم أنه صديد أو قيح من طريق غلبة الظن بإخبار الأطباء، أو علامة تغلب على ظن المبتلي يجب . وفي المنية روى عن محمد أنه قال الشيخ : إذا كان في عينه رمد وتسيل الدموع منها آمره بالوضوء لوقت كل صلاة، لأني أخاف أن يكون ما يسيل منها صديداً، فيكون صاحب عذر .اهـ. ونقل شارحها عن الكمال ما نقله عنه الشلبي، ثم قال شارحها ومما يشهد لهذا أي لكونه أمر استحباب ما في شرح الزاهدي عقيب هذه المسئلة، وعن هشام في جامعه إن كان قيحاً، فكالمستحاضة، وإلا فكالصحيحة، وأما قولهم : ا ماء الجرح والنفطة، وماء السرة ،والثدي والعين والأذن إن كان لعلة سواء ينبغي أن يحمل على ما إذا كان الخارج من العين متغيراً بسبب ذلك .اهـ. وفي الفتح عن التجنيس الغرب في العين إذا سال منه ماء نقض لأنه كالجرح وليس بدمع، وهو بالتحريك ورم في الماق اهـ وضبطه في الدر بفتح فسكون قال : وهو عرق في العين يسقي، ولا ينقطع اهـ قلت : وهل يجري في دمع العين الصافي ما جرى في ماء النفطة من الخلاف، والظاهر نعم لعدم الفرق. قال العارف بالله سيدي عبد الغني النابلسي : وينبغي أن يحكم برواية عدم النقض بالصافي الذي يخرج من النفطة في كي الحمصة، وإن ما يخرج منها لا ينقض وإن تجاوز إلى محل يلحقه حكم التطهير إذا كان ماءً صافياً أما غير الصافي بأن كان مخلوطاً بدم، أو قيح، أو صديد فإنه ناقض إذا وجد السيلان بأن تجاوز العصابة، وإلا لم ينقض ما دامت الورقة في موضع الكي معصبة بالعصابة وإن امتلات دماً، أو قيحاً ما لم يسل من حول العصابة، أو ينفذ منها دم، أو قيح سائل، وأما ظهوره من غير أن يتجاوزها فذلك من الجرح نفسه وهو غير ناقض ولو حل العصابة فأخرج الورقة والخرقة فوجد دماً أو قيحاً لولا الرباط لسال في غالب ظنه انتقض وضوءه في الحال لا قبل ذلك لكون النجاسة انفصلت عن موضعها أما قبل حلها فالنجاسة في موضعها لم تنفصل ولو لم يمكن قطع السيلان حقيقة أو حكماً كقطعه بالربط فهو معذور وإلا لا حتى لو كان لا يمتنع العذر بالربط أو الحشو وجب ذلك نقله السيد قوله : (وإن لم يتغير) أشار به إلى أنه لا فرق بين أنواع القيء سواء قاء من ساعته أم لا وقال الحسن : إذا تناول طعاماً أو ماء ثم قاء من ساعته لا ينتقض وضوءه لأنه طاهر حيث لم يستحل والذي اتصل به قليل قيء فلا يكون حدثاً فلا يكون نجساً وكذا الصبي إذا ارتضع وقاء من ساعته لا يكون نجساً والصحيح أنه حدث ونجس في الكل كما في الحلبي قيل وقول الحسن هو المختار سبعة هو سوداء محرقة (أو) مرة) أي صفراء والنقض بأحد هذه الأشياء (إذا ملأ الفم) لتنجسه بما في قعر المعدة وهو مذهب العشرة المبشرين بالجنة ولأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قاء فتوضأ قال الترمذي وهو أصح شيء في الباب ولقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم يعاد الوضوء من من أقطار البول والدم السائل والقيء ومن دسعة تملأ الفم ونوم مضطجع وقهقهة الرجل في الصلاة وخروج الدم (وهو) أي حد ملء الفم (ما لا يطبق عليه الفم إلا بتكلف على الأصح من التفاسير فيه وقيل ما يمنع الكلام (ويجمع) تقديراً (متفرق القيء إذا اتحد سببه) عند محمد وهو الأصح فينقض إن كان قدر ملء الفم وقال أبو يوسف إن اتحد المكان وماء فم النائم إن نزل من الرأس فهو طاهر اتفاقاً وكذا الصاعد من الجوف على المفتى به
(And the ear) if it is due to illness, on the correct view (and) nullifies it (vomiting of food or water) even if it did not change (or blood clot). And the meaning of 'tahrik' in language is mentioned by Allama Nuh and in al-Tabyin. And if one has ophthalmia or chronic conjunctivitis with tears flowing from it, they said: He is commanded to perform wudu for the time of every prayer due to the possibility that it may be pus or matter. Allama al-Shalabi said in his gloss: Shaykh Kamal al-Din said in the chapter on istihadah: I say this reasoning implies that it is a recommendatory command, because doubt and possibility regarding it being a nullifier do not necessitate the ruling of nullification, since certainty is not removed by doubt, and Allah knows best. Yes, if he knows that it is pus or matter through overwhelming probability by the report of physicians or a sign that overcomes the conjecture of the afflicted, it becomes obligatory. In al-Munya, it is narrated from Muhammad that he said: If one has ophthalmia and tears flow from it, I command him to perform wudu for the time of every prayer, because I fear that what flows from it may be pus, so he would be a person with an excuse. End. The commentator of al-Munya transmitted from al-Kamal what al-Shalabi transmitted from him, then said: Supporting this (i.e., that it is a recommendatory command) is what is in Sharh al-Zahidi after this issue, and from Hisham in his Jami': If it is pus, then like a mustahada; otherwise, like a healthy woman. As for their statement: 'Water of the wound and the blister, and water of the navel, breast, eye, and ear if for an ailment, it is the same' — it should be interpreted as when the discharge from the eye is altered due to that. End. In al-Fath, from al-Tajnis: Al-gharab (watery eye) in the eye if water flows from it, it nullifies because it is like a wound, not tears; it is by tahrīk 'swelling in the lacrimal gland'. End. Al-Durr recorded it with fatḥa then sukūn, saying: It is a vein in the eye that supplies water and does not stop. End. I say: Should the difference of opinion about the clear tear of the eye be the same as that about the water of the blister? Apparently yes, due to no distinction. Al-Arif billah Sayyidi Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said: It is appropriate to rule according to the narration of non-nullification by the clear water that comes out of the blister in cauterization of the bean, and that what comes out of it does not nullify even if it exceeds to a place that requires purification if it is clear water. As for not clear, if it is mixed with blood, pus, or matter, it is a nullifier if there is flowing, i.e., it exceeds the bandage; otherwise it does not nullify as long as the paper is in the place of cautery bandaged with the bandage even if filled with blood or pus, as long as it does not flow around the bandage or liquid blood or pus penetrates from it. As for its appearance without exceeding it, that is from the wound itself and is not a nullifier. If he loosens the bandage and removes the paper and cloth and finds blood or pus that would have flowed without the bandage in his dominant conjecture, his wudu is nullified immediately, not before that, because the impurity has separated from its place. Before loosening, the impurity is in its place and has not separated. If it is not possible to stop the flowing literally or effectively, like stopping it by bandaging, then he is excused; otherwise not, so that if he can avoid the excuse by bandaging or stuffing, it is obligatory. Transmitted by al-Sayyid. His saying: (even if it did not change) indicates that there is no difference between types of vomiting, whether one vomits immediately or not. Al-Hasan said: If one consumes food or water and then vomits immediately, his wudu is not nullified because it is pure since it has not changed, and what is connected to it is a small amount of vomit, so it is not an event, so it is not impure. Similarly, a child who breastfeeds and vomits immediately, it is not impure. The correct view is that it is an event and impure in all cases, as in al-Halabi. It is said that al-Hasan's view is the chosen one. Seven: it is black burnt (or bile), i.e., yellow, and nullification by one of these things (if it fills the mouth) because it becomes impure by what is in the bottom of the stomach, and it is the madhhab of the ten companions who were promised Paradise, and because the Prophet (peace be upon him) vomited and then performed wudu. Al-Tirmidhi said: This is the most authentic thing in this chapter, and due to his saying: 'Wudu is repeated from the margins of urine, flowing blood, and vomiting, and from a vomit that fills the mouth, and sleep lying down, and the laughter of a man in prayer, and the exit of blood.' (And its definition, i.e., 'filling the mouth' is that one cannot close the lips except with effort, according to the most correct of the interpretations; and it is said: what prevents speech. (And scattered vomit is collected) by estimation (if its cause is one) according to Muhammad, and it is the most correct, so it nullifies if it is the amount of a mouthful. Abu Yusuf said: If the place is one. And the water from the mouth of a sleeping person: if it descends from the head, it is pure by agreement; similarly, what ascends from the stomach according to the fatwa.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on Marāqī al-Falāḥ, which itself is a commentary on Nūr al-Īḍāḥ. The author, al-Tahtawi, begins by addressing the nullification of wudu by ear discharge when due to illness, affirming it as correct. He then moves to vomiting of food or water—even if unchanged—and blood clot (‎علق‎), stating these nullify wudu. The commentary expands on the precise nature of these nullifiers. Regarding eye discharge, a difference of opinion is noted: some scholars command wudu at each prayer time out of caution that the discharge might be pus, but al-Tahtawi clarifies this is recommendatory (istihbāb), not obligatory, because doubt does not override certainty. He outlines conditions for nullification: if it is known to be pus, obligation applies; otherwise, it is recommended. The discussion extends to water from wounds, blisters, and similar cases, with a detailed rule: clear water from a blister does not nullify, but if mixed with blood or pus, it nullifies if it flows beyond the bandage. Al-Tahtawi then addresses vomiting in depth, affirming that any type of vomit—food, water, black bile, yellow bile, or blood clot—nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth, based on a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). He defines 'filling the mouth' as an amount that prevents normal lip closure or speech. Regarding scattered vomit, he presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Muhammad, it is estimated if the cause (nausea) is one, aggregating to a mouthful; Abu Yusuf requires unity of place. The passage closes with a note on the water from a sleeping person's mouth: descending from the head is pure, ascending from the stomach follows the fatwa (considered impure and nullifying if it fills the mouth).

Key Terms
غرب (gharab): Watery eye, a condition where water flows from the eye due to a vein that supplies water continuously.
نفطة (nafṭa): Blister, a small pocket of fluid under the skin.
صديد (ṣadīd): Pus, a thick yellow or green fluid produced by infected tissue.
قيح (qayḥ): Matter, a thick fluid from a wound, similar to pus.
قيء (qay'): Vomiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on clear tears flowing from an infected eye?
A) They nullify wudu because the eye is infected.
B) They do not nullify wudu, but it is recommended to renew wudu.
C) They nullify wudu only if they exceed the eye socket.
D) They never nullify wudu.

Key Takeaway
Discharge from ear or eye nullifies wudu only when it is confirmed as pus or impurity; doubt does not break the state of purity.

intermediate
8 min
Vomiting: The Mouthful Threshold and Scattered Vomit
Commentary on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why vomiting a mouthful nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school, define 'mouthful', and apply the rule of aggregating scattered vomit.

Core Idea
A command to repeat wudu from vomiting indicates obligation; the threshold is a mouthful, and scattered vomit with a single cause is combined.

This section of the commentary focuses on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu. The author establishes that any vomit—food, water, bile, or blood clot—nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth. The evidence is a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who vomited and performed wudu. An intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded: how to treat scattered vomit? The preferred view is to estimate if the cause is one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items that nullify
Vomiting of food, water (even if unchanged), blood clot, black bile, and yellow bile all nullify wudu if they fill the mouth.
The type of vomit does not matter; the condition is the quantity.
2
Determine the threshold: mil' al-fam
Define 'mouthful' as an amount that prevents normal lip closure or speech. The commentary gives two interpretations: cannot close lips except with effort, or prevents speech. The first is most correct.
This is the key condition for nullification.
3
Analyze the dalil (proof)
The Prophet (peace be upon him) vomited and performed wudu, and the hadith commands: 'Wudu is repeated from... vomiting that fills the mouth.' This indicates obligation (wujub) by the Hanafi principle of command.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but binding in fiqh.
4
Address scattered vomit
If a person vomits multiple small amounts due to one cause (e.g., one bout of nausea), Imam Muhammad rules that they are aggregated to check if total reaches a mouthful. Abu Yusuf requires the vomits to occur in one place (majlis).
The preferred view (asahh) is Muhammad's.
5
Apply to water from a sleeping person's mouth
Water that descends from the head (e.g., saliva) is pure by consensus. Water that ascends from the stomach (vomit) is impure and nullifies if it fills the mouth, according to the fatwa.
This clarifies a common confusion.

الأصح فينقض إن كان قدر ملء الفم وقال أبو يوسف إن اتحد المكان وماء فم النائم إن نزل من الرأس فهو طاهر اتفاقاً وكذا الصاعد من الجوف على المفتى به
(And the ear) if it is due to illness, on the correct view (and) nullifies it (vomiting of food or water) even if it did not change (or blood clot). And the meaning of 'tahrik' in language is mentioned by Allama Nuh and in al-Tabyin. And if one has ophthalmia or chronic conjunctivitis with tears flowing from it, they said: He is commanded to perform wudu for the time of every prayer due to the possibility that it may be pus or matter. Allama al-Shalabi said in his gloss: Shaykh Kamal al-Din said in the chapter on istihadah: I say this reasoning implies that it is a recommendatory command, because doubt and possibility regarding it being a nullifier do not necessitate the ruling of nullification, since certainty is not removed by doubt, and Allah knows best. Yes, if he knows that it is pus or matter through overwhelming probability by the report of physicians or a sign that overcomes the conjecture of the afflicted, it becomes obligatory. In al-Munya, it is narrated from Muhammad that he said: If one has ophthalmia and tears flow from it, I command him to perform wudu for the time of every prayer, because I fear that what flows from it may be pus, so he would be a person with an excuse. End. The commentator of al-Munya transmitted from al-Kamal what al-Shalabi transmitted from him, then said: Supporting this (i.e., that it is a recommendatory command) is what is in Sharh al-Zahidi after this issue, and from Hisham in his Jami': If it is pus, then like a mustahada; otherwise, like a healthy woman. As for their statement: 'Water of the wound and the blister, and water of the navel, breast, eye, and ear if for an ailment, it is the same' — it should be interpreted as when the discharge from the eye is altered due to that. End. In al-Fath, from al-Tajnis: Al-gharab (watery eye) in the eye if water flows from it, it nullifies because it is like a wound, not tears; it is by tahrīk 'swelling in the lacrimal gland'. End. Al-Durr recorded it with fatḥa then sukūn, saying: It is a vein in the eye that supplies water and does not stop. End. I say: Should the difference of opinion about the clear tear of the eye be the same as that about the water of the blister? Apparently yes, due to no distinction. Al-Arif billah Sayyidi Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said: It is appropriate to rule according to the narration of non-nullification by the clear water that comes out of the blister in cauterization of the bean, and that what comes out of it does not nullify even if it exceeds to a place that requires purification if it is clear water. As for not clear, if it is mixed with blood, pus, or matter, it is a nullifier if there is flowing, i.e., it exceeds the bandage; otherwise it does not nullify as long as the paper is in the place of cautery bandaged with the bandage even if filled with blood or pus, as long as it does not flow around the bandage or liquid blood or pus penetrates from it. As for its appearance without exceeding it, that is from the wound itself and is not a nullifier. If he loosens the bandage and removes the paper and cloth and finds blood or pus that would have flowed without the bandage in his dominant conjecture, his wudu is nullified immediately, not before that, because the impurity has separated from its place. Before loosening, the impurity is in its place and has not separated. If it is not possible to stop the flowing literally or effectively, like stopping it by bandaging, then he is excused; otherwise not, so that if he can avoid the excuse by bandaging or stuffing, it is obligatory. Transmitted by al-Sayyid. His saying: (even if it did not change) indicates that there is no difference between types of vomiting, whether one vomits immediately or not. Al-Hasan said: If one consumes food or water and then vomits immediately, his wudu is not nullified because it is pure since it has not changed, and what is connected to it is a small amount of vomit, so it is not an event, so it is not impure. Similarly, a child who breastfeeds and vomits immediately, it is not impure. The correct view is that it is an event and impure in all cases, as in al-Halabi. It is said that al-Hasan's view is the chosen one. Seven: it is black burnt (or bile), i.e., yellow, and nullification by one of these things (if it fills the mouth) because it becomes impure by what is in the bottom of the stomach, and it is the madhhab of the ten companions who were promised Paradise, and because the Prophet (peace be upon him) vomited and then performed wudu. Al-Tirmidhi said: This is the most authentic thing in this chapter, and due to his saying: 'Wudu is repeated from the margins of urine, flowing blood, and vomiting, and from a vomit that fills the mouth, and sleep lying down, and the laughter of a man in prayer, and the exit of blood.' (And its definition, i.e., 'filling the mouth' is that one cannot close the lips except with effort, according to the most correct of the interpretations; and it is said: what prevents speech. (And scattered vomit is collected) by estimation (if its cause is one) according to Muhammad, and it is the most correct, so it nullifies if it is the amount of a mouthful. Abu Yusuf said: If the place is one. And the water from the mouth of a sleeping person: if it descends from the head, it is pure by agreement; similarly, what ascends from the stomach according to the fatwa.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on Marāqī al-Falāḥ, which itself is a commentary on Nūr al-Īḍāḥ. The author, al-Tahtawi, begins by addressing the nullification of wudu by ear discharge when due to illness, affirming it as correct. He then moves to vomiting of food or water—even if unchanged—and blood clot (‎علق‎), stating these nullify wudu. The commentary expands on the precise nature of these nullifiers. Regarding eye discharge, a difference of opinion is noted: some scholars command wudu at each prayer time out of caution that the discharge might be pus, but al-Tahtawi clarifies this is recommendatory (istihbāb), not obligatory, because doubt does not override certainty. He outlines conditions for nullification: if it is known to be pus, obligation applies; otherwise, it is recommended. The discussion extends to water from wounds, blisters, and similar cases, with a detailed rule: clear water from a blister does not nullify, but if mixed with blood or pus, it nullifies if it flows beyond the bandage. Al-Tahtawi then addresses vomiting in depth, affirming that any type of vomit—food, water, black bile, yellow bile, or blood clot—nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth, based on a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). He defines 'filling the mouth' as an amount that prevents normal lip closure or speech. Regarding scattered vomit, he presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Muhammad, it is estimated if the cause (nausea) is one, aggregating to a mouthful; Abu Yusuf requires unity of place. The passage closes with a note on the water from a sleeping person's mouth: descending from the head is pure, ascending from the stomach follows the fatwa (considered impure and nullifying if it fills the mouth).

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting, the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): An amount of vomit that fills the mouth such that one cannot close the lips except with effort, or that prevents speech.
علقة ('alaqa (pl. 'alaq)): Blood clot, a thick mass of blood.
سوداء (sawdā'): Black bile, one of the four humors, vomited as a dark fluid.
مرة (marra): Yellow bile, also impure and nullifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum amount of vomit that nullifies wudu?
A) Any amount, even a drop
B) An amount that fills the mouth (mil' al-fam)
C) An amount that colors the water if mixed
D) An amount that exceeds the throat

Key Takeaway
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if it amounts to a mouthful; scattered vomit with a single cause is combined to reach this threshold.
