Vomiting as a Nullifier of Wudu: The Mouthful Condition
Ruling with proof on vomiting nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for vomiting nullifying wudu (mil' al-fam) and apply the rule for scattered vomit when the cause is one.

Core Idea
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if it fills the mouth — mouthful is the decisive threshold.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu. The author of the text presents the key condition — that the vomit must fill the mouth — and provides proofs from the Sunnah. We will also explore an important intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding how to handle scattered vomit, and learn the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Vomiting (al-qay') is being ruled upon as a nullifier of wudu, but only when it meets the condition of being a mouthful.
2
Locate the condition (shart)
The condition is that the vomit must fill the mouth (mil' al-fam). Define what constitutes a mouthful: the mouth cannot close without difficulty, or it prevents speech.
The text provides two interpretations; the most correct is that the mouth cannot close over it except with difficulty.
3
Examine the dalil (proof)
Two hadith are cited: the Prophet (saw) vomiting and performing wudu (narrated by Tirmidhi), and the hadith listing nullifiers including 'a vomit that fills the mouth'. Understand how these prove the ruling.
The action of the Prophet and the imperative 'ya'adu al-wudu' indicate obligation (wujub), though the hadith is ahad so it is wajib not fard.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf differ on scattered vomit. Muhammad sums it if the cause is one (e.g., giddiness). Abu Yusuf requires unity of place (the session). The text states Muhammad's view is the most correct.
This affects whether multiple small vomits from the same cause add up to a mouthful.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Muhammad's: scattered vomit is summed if from one cause, and if the total equals a mouthful, wudu is nullified. The text declares this 'the most correct'.
This is the position for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
سبع
هو سوداء محرقة (أو) مرة) أي صفراء والنقض بأحد هذه الأشياء (إذا ملأ الفم) لتنجسه بما في قعر المعدة وهو مذهب العشرة المبشرين بالجنة ولأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قاء فتوضأ قال الترمذي وهو أصح شيء في الباب ولقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم يعاد الوضوء من من أقطار البول والدم السائل والقيء ومن دسعة تملأ الفم ونوم مضطجع وقهقهة الرجل في الصلاة وخروج الدم (وهو) أي حد ملء الفم (ما لا يطبق عليه الفم إلا بتكلف على الأصح من التفاسير فيه وقيل ما يمنع الكلام (ويجمع) تقديراً (متفرق القيء إذا اتحد سببه) عند محمد وهو الأصح فينقض إن كان قدر ملء الفم وقال أبو يوسف إن اتحد المكان وماء فم النائم إن نزل من الرأس فهو طاهر اتفاقاً وكذا الصاعد من الجوف على المفتى به وقيل إن كان أصفر أو منتناً فهو نجس
Book of Purification. Seven. It is black burnt (or) bitter (i.e., yellow) and nullification by one of these things (if it fills the mouth) because it is contaminated by what is at the bottom of the stomach, and it is the school of the ten given glad tidings of paradise, and because the Prophet (peace be upon him) vomited and performed wudu. Al-Tirmidhi said: It is the most authentic thing in this chapter. And due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Wudu is required from the edges of urine, flowing blood, vomit, a belch that fills the mouth, lying down sleep, laughter of a man in prayer, and emission of blood.' And (it) meaning the definition of mouthful: (what the mouth cannot close over except with difficulty) according to the most correct interpretations, and it is said: what prevents speech. (And) it is collected (by estimation) scattered vomit (if its cause is one) according to Muhammad, and it is the most correct, so it nullifies if it amounts to a mouthful. Abu Yusuf said: if the place is one. And the water of a sleeper's mouth if it descends from the head is pure by agreement, and similarly the ascendant from the stomach according to the mufta bihi, and it is said if it is yellow or foul-smelling then it is impure.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu. The author first defines the types of vomit: black burnt or yellow (bitter). The key condition for nullification is that the vomit fills the mouth (mil' al-fam). This is because the vomit is contaminated by stomach contents. The proof is from the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) vomiting and performing wudu, recorded by Tirmidhi, and a hadith listing various nullifiers. The definition of a mouthful is that the mouth cannot close over it without difficulty; it is also said that it prevents speech. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: regarding scattered vomit, if it originates from a single cause (like giddiness), it is summed together, and if the total equals a mouthful, it nullifies wudu. This is the view of Imam Muhammad (which is considered the most correct). Abu Yusuf, however, conditions the unity of place (i.e., the session). The passage also includes a related ruling on water from a sleeper's mouth: if it descends from the head (saliva), it is pure; if it ascends from the stomach (vomit), it is impure according to the fatwa, especially if yellow or foul-smelling.

Key Terms
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The amount of vomit that fills the mouth such that the mouth cannot close without difficulty.
القيء (al-qay'): Vomit, specifically the matter ejected from the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does vomiting nullify wudu?
A) Whenever any amount of vomit exits the stomach.
B) Only if the vomit fills the mouth to the extent that the mouth cannot close without difficulty.
C) Only if the vomit is black and burnt.
D) Only if the vomiting occurs during prayer.

Key Takeaway
Vomiting nullifies wudu only when the amount fills the mouth, with scattered vomit summed if from one cause.

Condition for Blood from the Mouth to Nullify Wudu
Ruling on mouth blood nullifying wudu with quantity condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for mouth blood nullifying wudu, apply the color-based test, and distinguish it from head and stomach blood rulings.

Core Idea
Mouth blood nullifies wudu only if it equals or overwhelms saliva—judged by color.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule on blood from a mouth wound as a nullifier of wudu. The key is not the mere presence of blood but its quantity relative to saliva. The school provides a practical color-based criterion to determine this. Additionally, the passage distinguishes between blood from the head and stomach, which have different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is blood emerging from a mouth wound. The ruling: it nullifies wudu.
Nullification is the hukm.
2
Apply the condition: ghalaba or musawa
The blood must be equal to or more than saliva. If saliva dominates, no nullification.
The text says 'ihtiyatan' (as a precaution), indicating the relied-upon position.
3
Use the color method for determination
Yellow indicates saliva dominant (no nullification). Redness indicates equality (nullification). Deep red indicates blood dominant (nullification).
This is a practical fiqh rule based on visual observation.
4
Distinguish from other blood sources
Blood from the head (e.g., nose) nullifies by mere flow, even if little, by consensus. Blood from the stomach (vomit-like) nullifies if it is fluid (not clotted).
These two categories have broader conditions than mouth blood.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i does not consider blood a wudu nullifier unless it flows from the two passages or is a large amount.
The Hanafi position is cautious (ihtiyatan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) ينقضه (دم) من جرح بفمه (غلب على البزاق) أي الريق (أو) ساواه) احتياطاً ويعلم باللون فالأصفر مغلوب وقيل الحمرة مساو وشديدها غالب والنازل من الرأس ناقض بسيلانه وإن قل بالإجماع وكذا الصاعد من الجوف رقيقاً وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ
(And) it is nullified by (blood) from a wound in his mouth (that overpowers the saliva) i.e., spittle (or equals it) as a precaution. And it is known by color: yellow is overpowered, and it is said redness is equal and intense redness is overpowering. And that which descends from the head nullifies by its flow even if little, by consensus. And similarly that which ascends from the stomach if it is fluid, and this is what most mashayikh adopted.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on blood from a mouth wound as a nullifier of wudu. The condition is that the blood must be dominant over the saliva, or at least equal to it, as a precaution. The determination is made by color: yellow indicates overpowered (saliva dominant), redness indicates equal, and deep red indicates overpowering (blood dominant). The passage also distinguishes between blood originating from the head (e.g., from the nose or mouth) which nullifies wudu by its very flow, even if little, by consensus of the Hanafi school. And blood ascending from the stomach (vomit-like) also nullifies if it is fluid (not clotted). This is the position adopted by the majority of mashayikh.

Key Terms
غلب على البزاق (ghalaba 'ala al-buzaq): Blood dominates saliva in quantity such that the color or quality indicates blood is more.
مغلوب (maghlub): Overpowered, i.e., the blood is less than saliva (saliva dominant).
مساو (musawi): Equal, i.e., blood and saliva are equal in amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does blood from a mouth wound nullify wudu?
A) Whenever any blood is seen.
B) Only when the blood is equal to or more than the saliva, determined by color.
C) Only if the blood flows from the head.
D) Only if the blood is from the stomach.

Key Takeaway
Mouth blood nullifies wudu only if it equals or overwhelms saliva, determined by color—a practical Hanafi precaution.

Sleep as a Nullifier of Wudu: The Hanafi Position and Its Proof
Ruling on sleep nullifying wudu with conditions and hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sleep nullifying wudu, identify the condition of firm seating, and explain the hadith proof and the Hanafi reasoning that sleep is an apparent cause (sabab) not a direct nullifier.

Core Idea
Sleep nullifies wudu only when the seat is not firmly on the ground; the hadith shows sleep loosens control, making wind release likely.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains the nuanced Hanafi position on sleep as a nullifier of wudu. Unlike simply stating that all sleep breaks wudu, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the posture of the sleeper and the underlying legal reasoning. The key is understanding that sleep itself is not a hadath but an occasioning cause (sabab) for the likely release of wind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the ruling on sleep and wudu? The text states that sleep nullifies wudu, meaning wudu becomes invalid and must be renewed.
The ruling is that wudu is broken by sleep under specific conditions.
2
Locate the condition for nullification
The nullification only occurs when 'the seat is not firmly on the ground' — i.e., when lying down, leaning, or on the back. Firm seated posture prevents nullification.
The condition is expressed as 'عدم تمكين المقعدة من الأرض'.
3
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith: 'العينان وكاء السه فإذا نامت العينان انطلق الوكاء' (The eyes are the tie of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the tie is loosened). This indicates that sleep causes loss of control over gas release.
The hadith is ahad, thus ruling is wajib, not fard.
4
Trace the Hanafi legal reasoning
Sleep is not a direct nullifier (hadath). The actual nullifier is the release of wind (hadath), but since it is unavoidable during deep sleep, sleep is treated as the apparent cause (sabab) and is substituted in the ruling for practical ease.
This is a type of istihsan or legal fiction to simplify application.
5
Note the exceptions
Light drowsiness (nu'as) where one can still hear does not nullify wudu, because control is not fully lost.
This differentiates Hanafi from Shafi'i which nullifies all sleep.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) ينقضه (نوم) وهو فترة طبيعية تحدث فتمنع الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة عن العمل بسلامتها وعن استعمال العقل مع قيامه وهذا إذا لم تتمكن فيه المقعدة) يعني المخرج (من الأرض باضطجاع وتورك واستلقاء على القفا ولو كان مريضاً يصلي بالإيماء على الصحيح وانقلاب على الوجه لزوال المسكة والناقض الحدث للإشارة إليه بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم العينان وكاء السه فإذا نامت العينان انطلق الوكاء وبه التنبيه على أن الناقض ليس النوم لأنه ليس حدثاً وإنما الحدث ما لا يخلو عنه النائم فأقيم السبب الظاهر مقامه والنعاس الخفيف الذي يسمع به ما يقال عنده لا ينقض
(And) it is nullified by (sleep) which is a natural period that occurs, preventing the external and internal senses from functioning properly and from using the intellect while it (the sleep) persists. And this is when his seat (i.e., the anus) is not firmly placed on the ground by lying down, sitting with thighs apart, or lying on the back, even if he is sick praying with gestures according to the correct view, or turning onto the face because the hold is lost. And the actual nullifier is the hadath, as indicated by his saying (peace be upon him): 'The eyes are the tie of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the tie is loosened.' And this indicates that sleep itself is not a nullifier because it is not a hadath, but rather what is unavoidable during sleep (i.e., passing wind) is the nullifier; thus the apparent cause (sleep) is placed in its stead. Light drowsiness in which one hears what is said does not nullify.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage deals with sleep as a nullifier of wudu. The author first defines sleep: a natural state that prevents the senses and intellect from working normally. The key condition for nullification is that the person is not in a firm seated position: i.e., if they are lying down, resting on their thighs, or on their back, then sleep nullifies wudu. Even a sick person praying with gestures is included. However, if one is seated firmly on the ground such that the anus is pressed against the earth, sleep does not nullify because any passing of gas would be detected. The proof is the hadith: 'The eyes are the tie of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the tie is loosened.' This indicates that sleep itself is not the nullifier; rather, it is the likely release of wind (hadath) that occurs during sleep. Therefore, sleep is treated as the apparent cause. Light drowsiness (nu'as) in which one can still hear what is said does not nullify wudu.

Key Terms
وكاء (wika'): A tie or string; metaphorically, the control over the release of gas from the anus.
النعاس الخفيف (al-nu'as al-khafif): Light drowsiness where one can still hear what is said; does not nullify wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does sleep NOT nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) When sleeping on the back.
B) When sleeping while firmly seated on the ground with the anus pressed.
C) When sleeping on the stomach.
D) When sleeping in any position.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, sleep nullifies wudu only when the seat is not firmly on the ground, based on the hadith that sleep loosens the 'tie' of the anus, making wind release likely.

Blood from Mouth Wound Nullifying Wudu
Ruling on blood from mouth wound with color indicators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when blood from a mouth wound nullifies wudu and apply the color indicators to determine nullification.

Core Idea
Blood nullifies wudu only if it overcomes or equals saliva; color reveals the ratio.

This lesson explains a specific ruling of wudu nullification: blood from a mouth wound. The key is the ratio of blood to saliva, which can be judged by colour. The Hanafi school provides a practical method using colour indicators, and also distinguishes this from other types of impurities (head, stomach).

Guided Path
1
Identify the nullifying agent
The text specifies blood from a wound in the mouth. This blood, if it exits the mouth mixed with saliva, may nullify wudu.
Not all mouth impurities nullify; only blood that overcomes saliva.
2
Understand the condition of overcoming
The blood must be ghalib (overcoming) or musawin (equal) to the saliva. If the saliva overcomes (maghlub), it does not nullify. Equality is treated as nullifying out of precaution.
The ruling depends on the relative quantity.
3
Apply the color indicator
Yellowish discharge indicates blood is overcome (no nullification); red (musawin) indicates equality (nullifies); deep red/intense red (ghalib) indicates blood overcomes (nullifies).
Color is a practical guide for the average person.
4
Compare with other nullifiers
The text also states that blood descending from the head (e.g., nosebleed) nullifies by consensus even if a small amount, and thin vomit from the stomach nullifies according to general mashayikh.
These are separate rulings; focus on mouth wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفتي به وقيل إن كان أصفر أو منتناً فهو نجس (و) ينقضه (دم) من جرح بفمه (غلب على البزاق) أي الريق (أو) ساواه) احتياطاً ويعلم باللون فالأصفر مغلوب وقيل الحمرة مساو وشديدها غالب والنازل من الرأس ناقض بسيلانه وإن قل بالإجماع وكذا الصاعد من الجوف رقيقاً وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ
The relied-upon position, and it is said if it is yellow or putrid then it is najis. And it is nullified by blood from a wound in his mouth that overcomes saliva, i.e., spittle, or equals it as a precaution. It is known by color: yellow is overcome, and it is said redness is equal and its intense is overcoming. And that which descends from the head is a nullifier by its flow even if small by consensus, and likewise that which ascends from the stomach thin, and the general mashayikh adopted this.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on when blood from a mouth wound nullifies wudu. The actual nullifier is the blood that overcomes the saliva; if the saliva overcomes the blood, it does not nullify. As a precaution, if they are equal, it also nullifies. The color indicates the ratio: yellow means the blood is overcome by saliva, redness indicates equality, and deep red indicates blood is overcoming. Blood flowing from the head (like nosebleed) nullifies even if a little, by consensus, and thin vomit from the stomach also nullifies according to the general mashayikh.

Key Terms
غالب (ghālib): Overcoming; the blood being more than the saliva.
مساو (musāwin): Equal; blood and saliva equal in amount, treated as nullifier by precaution.
مغلوب (maghlūb): Overcome; the blood being less than the saliva, so no nullification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is nullified as a precaution.
B) It is not nullified because saliva overcomes.
C) It is nullified only if the blood is yellow.
D) It is nullified only if the blood flows from the head.

Key Takeaway
Blood from a mouth wound nullifies wudu only if it overcomes or equals the saliva; the color of the discharge indicates the ratio.

Sleep as a Nullifier of Wudu: Ruling and Proof
Ruling and proof on sleep nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sleep and wudu, explain why sleep itself is not a nullifier, and distinguish between light and heavy sleep based on the hadith.

Core Idea
Sleep does not nullify wudu — only the hadath that may occur during it does.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on sleep as a nullifier of wudu. The key distinction is between sleep itself and the event (hadath) that may accompany heavy sleep. We will analyze the hadith of the 'drawstring' to understand why the school holds that sleep is merely a cause, not a nullifier.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
Identify the legal ruling on sleep: heavy sleep nullifies wudu under specific conditions, while light slumber does not. Note the difference between sleep as a cause and the actual nullifier (hadath).
2
Identify the Hadith proof
Locate the hadith cited by the author: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the drawstring is released.' Understand the metaphor of the drawstring (wikā’) and its implication.
Hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith indicates that the nullifier is the emission (release of the drawstring), not sleep itself. The hadith links the nullification to the action of the eyes sleeping, which leads to relaxation of the sphincter.
The phrase 'انطلق الوكاء' (the drawstring is released) implies something exits.
4
Distinguish types of sleep
Categorize sleep into light slumber (nu’ās), where the person still hears, and heavy sleep, where the base is not on the ground and control is lost. Only heavy sleep is a cause for potential nullification.
Light slumber does not nullify even if the person hears nothing according to some scholars.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Acknowledge that some early Hanafis considered sleep itself a nullifier, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that sleep is only a cause. This distinction affects cases like a person with flatulence who sleeps without emission.
Al-Zayla'i and others preferred the view that sleep is not a nullifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقضه (نوم) وهو فترة طبيعية تحدث فتمنع الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة عن العمل بسلامتها وعن استعمال العقل مع قيامه وهذا إذا لم تتمكن فيه المقعدة) يعني المخرج (من الأرض باضطجاع وتورك واستلقاء على القفا ولو كان مريضاً يصلي بالإيماء على الصحيح وانقلاب على الوجه لزوال المسكة والناقض الحدث للإشارة إليه بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم العينان وكاء السه فإذا نامت العينان انطلق الوكاء وبه التنبيه على أن الناقض ليس النوم لأنه ليس حدثاً وإنما الحدث ما لا يخلو عنه النائم فأقيم السبب الظاهر مقامه والنعاس الخفيف الذي يسمع به ما يقال عنده لا ينقض. (العينان وكاء السه) قال في النهاية : أصل سه سته بوزن فرس وجمعه أستاه كأفراس فحذفت الهاء وعوّضت عنها الهمزة فقيل است فإذا ردت الهاء وهي لامها وحذفت العين التي هي التاء انحذفت الهمزة التي جيء بها عوضاً عن الهاء فقيل: سه بفتح السين ويروى في الحديث وكاء الست اهـ وفي قوله العينان وكاء السه تشبيه بليغ بفم الزق على طريق الاستعارة بالكناية وإثبات الوكاء له تخييل واستعمال العينين في اليقظة مجاز مرسل علاقته التلازم لأنه يلزم من انفتاحهما اليقظة وحمل الوكاء على العيني من التشبيه البليغ سواء كانا بمعنى اليقظة أو أبقيا على معناهما أو من باب الكناية أي اليقظة أو العينان كرباط الدبر اهـ مدابغي في حاشيته على الخطيب وإعرابه بالحركات على الهاء لأنها لام الكلمة قوله : (وإنما الحدث ما لا يخلو عنه النائم) صححه في السراج واختاره الزيلعي مقتصراً عليه وحكي في التوشيح الإتفاق عليه، وتفرع على الخلاف ما ذكره العلامة الشلبي في حاشية الزيلعي ونصه سئلت عن شيخ به إنفلات ريح : هل ينتقض وضوءه بالنوم، فأجبت بعدم النقض بناء على ما هو الصحيح، أن النوم نفسه ليس بناقض، وإن الناقض ما يخرج ومن ذهب إلى أن النوم نفسه ناقض لزمه نقض وضوء من به إنفلات ريح بالنوم والله تعالى أعلم .اهـ. قوله : (الذي يسمع به الباء بمعنى مع، وقوله : ما يقال : أي أكثر ما يقال قال في الخانية : النعاس لا ينقض الوضوء وهو قليل نو أكثر ما يقال، ويجري عنده .اهـ وظاهر المصنف كالخانية أنه لا يشترط الفهم، والذي في الفتح عن الدقاق والرازي إن كان لا يفهم عامة ما قيل عنده كان حدثاً وإن كان لا يفهم حرفاً، أو حرفين يعني كلمة، أو كلمتين لا اهـ. ويظهر الفرق بين العبارتين في سماع غير لغته، والظاهر إعتبار السماع فقط. تنبيه : لا نقض من الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، فلا يحتاج أن يقال: نومهم غير ناقض. وإلا فهو الثقيل ناقض
And it is nullified by sleep, which is a natural period that occurs preventing the external and internal senses from working with their soundness and from using the intellect while it exists. This applies when the base (meaning the anus) is not firmly placed on the ground by lying down, leaning on one side, or lying on the back, even if a sick person prays by gesture according to the correct view, and turning onto the face due to loss of control. The nullifier is the hadath, indicated by his saying, peace be upon him: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the drawstring is released.' And by this it is indicated that the nullifier is not sleep because it is not a hadath; rather, the hadath is what the sleeper cannot avoid. So the apparent cause is established in its place. Light slumber in which one hears what is said does not nullify. ... (linguistic explanation of the hadith) ... And if not, then it is heavy and nullifies.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on sleep as a nullifier of wudu. It defines sleep as a natural state that disables the senses but does not remove consciousness. The Hanafi school distinguishes between heavy sleep (where one loses awareness) and light slumber. The proof is the hadith that the eyes are the drawstring of the anus, meaning that when one sleeps, the sphincter relaxes, potentially releasing gas. Importantly, the hadith shows that sleep itself is not the nullifier; rather, it is the event (hadath) that occurs during sleep. Therefore, a person who has flatulence (enflation of wind) does not have his wudu nullified by sleep, because the nullifier only happens if something actually exits. Light slumber where one still hears does not nullify. The prophets, peace be upon them, are excluded from nullification by sleep because of their special status. The relied-upon position is that only heavy sleep nullifies.

Key Terms
نوم (nawm): A natural state that prevents the senses from working while consciousness persists; categorized as light (nā'is) or heavy.
النعاس (an-nu'ās): Light slumber where one can still hear what is said; does not nullify wudu.
الوكاء (al-wikā'): Drawstring or tie; metaphorically the control of the anus by the eyes being awake.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following best describes the role of sleep in nullifying wudu?
A) Sleep is a nullifier because it relaxes the body.
B) Sleep is a cause for a potential nullifier, but not the nullifier itself.
C) Sleep nullifies only if the person is lying down.
D) Light sleep nullifies even if nothing exits.

Key Takeaway
Sleep does not nullify wudu; only the unseen event (hadath) that may occur during heavy sleep does.

Raising the Buttocks While Sleeping: Nullification of Wudu
Ruling on nullification of wudu by raising the base while sleeping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raising the buttocks during sleep and explain the condition of continuous sleep according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Raising the buttocks from the ground while sleeping nullifies wudu, even without falling, because the base loses contact.

Sleep nullifies wudu when it is deep enough that the person loses awareness. But what if the person remains sitting upright but the buttocks rise? This lesson unpacks a specific ruling from the Hanafi school that addresses this subtle case. The passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies that even without falling, the mere raising of the base from the ground nullifies wudu, because it indicates a loss of control. Imam Abu Hanifa further distinguishes between interrupted sleep and continuous sleep.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is raising the buttocks (maq'adah) while sleeping sitting on the ground. The ruling is that this nullifies wudu.
The term 'maq'adah' refers to the anus area that contacts the ground.
2
Understand the condition of 'raising the base'
The base leaving the ground indicates deep sleep, even if the person does not fall over. The nullification occurs because the base has left, not because of falling.
Falling is not a condition; merely lifting the buttocks suffices.
3
Note the distinction between falling and not falling
The apparent view: nullification occurs even without falling. Some may think falling is necessary, but the text clarifies it is not.
This is the explicit statement in the matn: 'even if he does not fall on the ground'.
4
Learn Imam Abu Hanifa's addition about waking
Imam Abu Hanifa added a condition: if the person wakes up each time he falls (i.e., sleep is not continuous), then wudu is not nullified; but if he remains sleeping after falling and then wakes up, it is nullified because he has slept in a lying posture.
This is an intra-Hanafi detail that affects the ruling in practice.
5
Identify the relied-upon position
The fatwa is on the apparent view (raising base nullifies) as stated in al-Tabyin and al-Fath, and it is the position affirmed in al-Siraj.
The mufta bihi is the more cautious view, warning against potential nullification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقضه (ارتفاع مقعدة) قاعد (نائم) على الأرض (قبل انتباهه وإن لم يسقط على الأرض (في الظاهر) من المذهب لزوال المقعدة. قوله: (وينقضه إرتفاع مقعدة الخ) فقيل إن انتبه كلما سقط، فلا ينتقض وإن استقر نائماً، ثم انتبه إنتقض لوجود النوم مضطجعاً هذا قول الإمام. قال في التبيين، وهو الظاهر وفي الفتح، وعليه الفتوى وفي المضمرات عن الزاد وهو الصحيح في رواية الحسن وبه جزم في السراج
And it is nullified by the raising of the base of a sleeping person sitting on the ground before he wakes up, even if he does not fall on the ground, in the apparent position of the school, because the base has left. His words: 'And it is nullified by the raising of the base, etc.' So it is said: if he wakes up each time he falls, then it is not nullified; and if he remains sleeping then wakes up, it is nullified due to the existence of sleep lying down. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. It is said in al-Tabyin that this is the apparent view, and in al-Fath the fatwa is upon it, and in al-Mudmarat from al-Zad it is the correct view in the narration of Hasan, and al-Siraj affirmed it.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case: when a sleeping person who is sitting upright raises his buttocks from the ground before waking up. The apparent position of the Hanafi school is that this nullifies wudu, even if he does not fall, because the base (the anus area) loses its firm contact with the ground, which indicates deep sleep. The commentary elaborates on a detail from Imam Abu Hanifa: if the person wakes up each time he falls over (i.e., his sleep is not continuous), then his wudu is not nullified; but if he remains sleeping after falling and then wakes up, his wudu is nullified because he has slept in a lying position. This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

Key Terms
مقعدة (maq'adah): The base, meaning the buttocks or the anus area; the part in contact with the ground when sitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His wudu is not nullified because he did not fall.
B) His wudu is nullified because his base left the ground.
C) His wudu is nullified only if he falls.
D) His wudu is nullified only if he wakes up after falling.

Key Takeaway
The apparent Hanafi view: raising the buttocks from the ground during sleep nullifies wudu, even without falling, because it indicates deep sleep and loss of control.
